The drop in cases was believed to be related to an integrated strategy combining isolation and treatment with community behaviour change, including safe burial practices, case finding and [[contact tracing]].<ref>{{cite news |last=Cumming-Bruce |first=Nick |date=29 October 2014 |title=Ebola Slowing in Liberia, WHO Says, but International Support Is Still Necessary |url=https://www.nytimes.com/2014/10/30/world/africa/ebola-liberia-who.html |newspaper=The New York Times |access-date=3 November 2014 }}</ref><ref>{{cite news|title=Ebola state of emergency lifted in Liberia|url=http://www.cbsnews.com/news/ebola-vaccine-trials-to-begin-in-west-africa-amid-threat-of-mali-outbreak/|work=CBS News|date=14 November 2014|access-date=14 November 2014}}</ref> [[Roselyn Nugba-Ballah]], leader of the Safe & Diginified Burial Practices Team during the crisis, was awarded the Florence Nightingale Medal in 2017 for her work during the crisis.<ref>{{Cite web|title="A True Patriot," Nurse Roselyn Ballah, Gets Nightingale Award Presented|url=https://www.liberianobserver.com/news/a-true-patriot-nurse-roselyn-ballah-gets-nightingale-award-presented/|last=Harmon|first=William Q.|date=2017-12-01|website=Liberian Observer|language=en-US|access-date=2020-05-23|archive-date=14 April 2021|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210414232122/https://www.liberianobserver.com/news/a-true-patriot-nurse-roselyn-ballah-gets-nightingale-award-presented/|url-status=dead}}</ref>

In January 2015, the MSF field coordinator reported that Liberia was down to only 5&nbsp;confirmed cases.<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.abc.net.au/news/2015-01-29/worlds-largest-ebola-unit-dismantled-as-outbreak-retreats/6053498|title=Ebola outbreak eases as world's largest treatment centre dismantled|work=ABC News|access-date=29 January 2015|date=2015-01-29}}</ref>  In March, after two weeks of not reporting any new cases, 3&nbsp;new cases were confirmed.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.healthmap.org/site/diseasedaily/article/three-new-ebola-cases-liberia-32715|title=Three New Ebola Cases in Liberia|publisher=The Disease Daily|date=27 March 2015|access-date=28 March 2015|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150330072625/http://www.healthmap.org/site/diseasedaily/article/three-new-ebola-cases-liberia-32715|archive-date=30 March 2015|url-status=dead}}</ref>  On 8 April, a new health minister was named in an effort to end Ebola in the country and on 26 April, MSF handed the Ebola treatment facility, ELWA-3, over to the government.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://allafrica.com/stories/201504271412.html|title=allAfrica.com: Liberia: MSF Hands Over ELWA – 3 to Liberian Ministry of Health|publisher=allAfrica.com|access-date=29 April 2015}}</ref>  On 30 April, the US shut down a special Ebola treatment unit in Liberia.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://abcnews.go.com/Health/wireStory/us-shuts-ebola-treatment-center-liberia-30703643|title=US Shuts Down Ebola Treatment Center in Liberia|work=ABC News|access-date=2 May 2015}}</ref>  The last known case of Ebola died on 27 March,<ref>{{cite web|url=https://news.yahoo.com/liberia-names-health-minister-seeks-beat-ebola-144957753.html|title=Liberia names new health minister as it seeks to beat Ebola|date=8 April 2015|publisher=Yahoo! News|access-date=16 April 2015}}</ref> and the country was officially declared Ebola-free on 9 May 2015, after 42&nbsp;days without any further cases being recorded.  The WHO congratulated Liberia saying, "reaching this milestone is a testament to the strong leadership and coordination of Liberian President Ellen Johnson Sirleaf and the Liberian Government, the determination and vigilance of Liberian communities, the extensive support of global partners, and the tireless and heroic work of local and international health teams."<ref name="auto1">{{cite report|url=https://www.who.int/features/2015/liberia-ends-ebola/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150510104510/http://www.who.int/features/2015/liberia-ends-ebola/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=10 May 2015|title= How Liberia got to zero cases of Ebola|date= 9 May 2015|publisher= WHO|access-date= 10 May 2015}}</ref>  As at May 2015, the country remained on high alert against recurrence of the disease.<ref>{{cite news|url = https://www.nytimes.com/2015/05/10/world/africa/liberia-is-free-of-ebola-world-health-organization-declares.html|title = Liberia Is Declared Free of Ebola, but Officials Sound Note of Caution|date = 9 May 2015|newspaper= The New York Times|last = Fink|first = Sheri}}</ref><ref>{{cite report |url=http://apps.who.int/iris/bitstream/10665/161244/1/roadmapsitrep_8Apr2015_eng.pdf?ua=1 |title=Ebola situation report |publisher=WHO |date=8 April 2015 |format=PDF}}</ref><ref>{{cite report|url=http://apps.who.int/iris/bitstream/10665/164523/1/roadmapsitrep_6May15_eng.pdf?ua=1|title=Ebola situation report |publisher=WHO |date=6 May 2015 |format=PDF}}</ref>

After three months with no new reports of cases, on 29 June Liberia reported that the body of a 17-year-old boy, who had been treated for malaria, tested positive for Ebola.  The WHO said the boy had been in close contact with at least 200 people,<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.nytimes.com/2015/07/04/world/africa/liberia-200-reportedly-had-contact-with-ebola-victim.html?_r=0 |title=Liberia: 200 Reportedly Had Contact With Ebola Victim |newspaper=The New York Times |author=Gladstone, Rick |date=3 July 2015}}</ref> who they were following up, and that "the case reportedly had no recent history of travel, contact with visitors from affected areas, or funeral attendance."  A second case was confirmed on 1 July.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.npr.org/sections/goatsandsoda/2015/06/30/418913144/ebola-returns-to-liberia-with-a-mysterious-case-near-monrovia|title=Ebola Returns To Liberia With A Mysterious Case Near Monrovia|date=30 June 2015|newspaper=NPR |access-date=1 July 2015|last1=Doucleff|first1=Michaeleen}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.nbcnews.com/health/health-news/liberia-reports-second-case-ebola-n385116|title=Liberia Reports Second Case of Ebola|date=1 July 2015|work=NBC News}}</ref>  After a third new case was confirmed on 2 July, and it was discovered that all 3 new cases had shared a meal of [[dog meat]], researchers looked at the possibility that the meat may have been involved in the transfer of the virus.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-liberia-idUSKCN0PC0WJ20150702|title=Liberia investigating animal link after Ebola re-emerges|date=2 July 2015|work=Reuters}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://pulse.com.gh/news/in-liberia-dog-meat-identified-as-possible-source-of-ebola-id3931731.html|title=In Liberia: Dog meat identified as possible source of Ebola|author=Sena Quashie|date=2 July 2015|publisher=pulse.com.gh|access-date=4 July 2015}}</ref>  Testing of the dog's remains, however, was negative for the Ebola virus.<ref>Reuters. (7 July 2015).[https://www.nytimes.com/2015/07/08/world/africa/liberia-dog-suspected-in-new-ebola-case-tests-negative-for-the-virus.html?_r=0 "Liberia: Dog Suspected in New Ebola Case Tests Negative for the Virus"]. ''The New York Times''.</ref>  By 9 July 3 more cases were discovered, bringing the total number of new cases to 5, all from the same area.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://abcnews.go.com/Health/wireStory/liberia-confirms-ebola-cases-32340305|title=Liberia Confirms 2 New Ebola Cases|date=9 July 2015|work=ABC News|access-date=9 July 2015}}</ref>  On 14 July, a woman died of the disease in the county of [[Montserrado]], bringing the total to 6.<ref>{{cite magazine|url=http://time.com/3957212/ebola-liberia-reemerge/|title=Ebola|author=Tanya Basu|magazine=Time |date=14 July 2015}}</ref>  On 20 July, the last patients were discharged,<ref>Reuters. (20 July 2015).[https://www.nytimes.com/2015/07/21/world/africa/liberia-ebola-patients-discharged-last-of-the-countrys-latest-wave.html?_r=0 "Liberia: Ebola Patients Discharged, Last of the Country's Latest Wave"]. ''The New York Times''.</ref> and on 3 September 2015, Liberia was declared Ebola-free again.<ref name="Lib_free_3_9">{{cite report|url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/statements/2015/ebola-transmission-over-liberia/en/ |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150905034754/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/statements/2015/ebola-transmission-over-liberia/en/ |url-status=dead |archive-date=5 September 2015 |title=Ebola transmission in Liberia over: Nation enters 90-day intensive surveillance period |publisher=WHO |date=3 September 2015}}</ref>

After two months of being Ebola-free, a new case was confirmed on 20 November 2015, when a 15-year-old boy was diagnosed with the virus<ref name="Liberia New Case 20/11/2015 LA Times">{{cite news|url=http://www.latimes.com/world/africa/la-fg-ebola-case-in-liberia-20151120-story.html |title=New Ebola case confirmed in Liberia |newspaper=Los Angeles Times |date=2015-11-20 |access-date=2017-01-15}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://allafrica.com/stories/201511200980.html|title=Liberia: Fears of Deadly Ebola Virus Reportedly Resurface in Liberia|publisher=allAfrica.com|access-date=21 November 2015}}</ref> and two family members subsequently tested positive as well.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.nbcnews.com/storyline/ebola-virus-outbreak/ebola-returns-liberia-again-n467146|title=Ebola Returns to Liberia, Again|author=Maggie Fox|work=NBC News|access-date=21 November 2015}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/nov/22/liberian-health-officials-ebola-outbreak-monrovia|title=Liberian health officials move to control Ebola outbreak in Monrovia|newspaper=The Guardian|date=22 November 2015|access-date=23 November 2015}}</ref>  Health officials were concerned because the child had not recently travelled or been exposed to someone with Ebola and the WHO stated that "we believe that this is probably again, somehow, someone who has come in contact with a virus that had been persisting in an individual, who had suffered the disease months ago." Two staff of the CDC were sent to the country to help ascertain the cause of the new cases.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.nytimes.com/aponline/2015/11/23/world/africa/ap-af-liberia-ebola.html|title=Liberia Seeks US Help to Determine Cause of New Ebola Cases|date=23 November 2015|agency=Associated Press|access-date=24 November 2015}}</ref>  The infected boy died on 24 November,<ref>{{cite web|url=https://abcnews.go.com/Health/wireStory/liberia-records-1st-ebola-death-july-35386693|title=Liberia Records 1st Ebola Death Since July|work=ABC News|access-date=26 November 2015}}</ref> and on 3 December 2 remaining cases were released after recovering from the disease.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://news.yahoo.com/liberias-last-two-ebola-patients-recover-leave-hospital-193116450.html|title=Liberia's last two Ebola patients recover, leave hospital|date=3 December 2015|publisher=Yahoo! News|access-date=4 December 2015}}</ref> The 42-day countdown toward Liberia being declared Ebola-free, for the third time, started on 4 December 2015.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-liberia-idUSKBN0TU2Q820151211#hJsbSReZzDZ7Ih4S.97|title=Liberia's last Ebola contacts finish quarantine|author=Reuters Editorial|date=11 December 2015|work=Reuters|access-date=12 December 2015}}</ref> On 16 December, the WHO reaffirmed that the cases in Liberia were the result of re-emergence of the virus in a previously infected person,<ref>{{cite report|url=http://apps.who.int/ebola/current-situation/ebola-situation-report-16-december-2015|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20151218125819/http://apps.who.int/ebola/current-situation/ebola-situation-report-16-december-2015|url-status=dead|archive-date=18 December 2015|title=Ebola Situation Report |date= 16 December 2015|publisher=WHO |access-date=25 December 2015}}</ref> and there was speculation that the boy may have been infected by an individual who became infectious once more due to pregnancy, which may have weakened her immune system.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-liberia-idUSKBN0U02EJ20151217|title=Female survivor may be cause of Ebola flare-up in Liberia|author1=Emma Farge |author2=James Harding Giahyue |date=17 December 2015|work=Reuters|access-date=22 December 2015}}</ref> On 18 December, the WHO indicated that it still considered Ebola in Western Africa a public health emergency, though progress had been made.<ref>{{cite report|url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/statements/2015/ihr-ebola-8th-meeting/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20151222223327/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/statements/2015/ihr-ebola-8th-meeting/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=22 December 2015|title=Statement on the 8th meeting of the IHR Emergency Committee regarding the Ebola outbreak in West Africa|publisher=WHO|access-date=23 December 2015}}</ref>

After having completed the 42-day time period, Liberia was declared free from the virus on 14 January 2016, effectively ending the outbreak that had started in neighbouring Guinea 2 years earlier. Liberia began a 90-day period of heightened surveillance, scheduled to conclude on 13 April 2016,<ref>{{cite magazine|url=http://time.com/4179412/ebola-outbreak-over/|title=West Africa to Be Declared Ebola-Free, Health Officials Say|author=Alexandra Sifferlin|magazine=Time|access-date=14 January 2016}}</ref> but on 1 April, it was reported that a new Ebola fatality had occurred,<ref name="reu2016">{{cite news|url=http://news.trust.org/item/20160401113911-cthc6|title=Liberia records new Ebola death, months after end of its outbreak|first=Thomson Reuters|last=Foundation|agency=Reuters |access-date=9 June 2016}}</ref> and on 3 April, a second case was reported in [[Monrovia]].<ref name="abc">{{cite web|url=https://abcnews.go.com/International/wireStory/ebola-free-liberia-confirms-2nd-case-38116197 |title=International News &#124; World News |work=ABC News |access-date=2017-01-15}}</ref> On 4 April, it was reported that 84 individuals were under observation due to contact with the 2 confirmed Ebola cases.<ref>{{cite report|url=https://www.who.int/csr/disease/ebola/liberia-flareups-update/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160408074558/http://www.who.int/csr/disease/ebola/liberia-flareups-update/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=8 April 2016|title=Emergency response to Ebola flare underway in Liberia. Case investigation widens to Guinea|publisher=WHO |access-date=9 June 2016}}</ref> By 7 April, Liberia had confirmed 3 new cases since the virus resurfaced and a total of 97&nbsp;contacts, including 15&nbsp;healthcare workers, were being monitored.<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.thenewdawnliberia.com/news/10041-liberia-records-97-ebola-contacts|title=Liberia records 97 Ebola contacts|publisher=The New Dawn Liberia|access-date=7 April 2016|date=7 April 2016|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160409014448/http://www.thenewdawnliberia.com/news/10041-liberia-records-97-ebola-contacts|archive-date=9 April 2016|df=dmy-all}}</ref> The index case of the new flareup was reported to be the wife of a patient who died from Ebola in Guinea; she had travelled to Monrovia after the funeral but succumbed to the disease.<ref>{{cite report|url=https://www.who.int/csr/disease/ebola/liberia-guinea-flareups-update/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160408225351/http://www.who.int/csr/disease/ebola/liberia-guinea-flareups-update/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=8 April 2016|title=Liberia and Guinea step up coordination to stem new cases of Ebola|publisher=WHO|access-date=7 April 2016|date=8 April 2016}}</ref> The outbreak in Guinea, in turn, had begun when a man, who had survived Ebola, had sexual intercourse with a woman and passed the virus to her, even though he had recovered more than a year earlier.<ref name=lurked/><ref name=BDiallo/>

On 29 April, WHO reported that Liberia had discharged the last patient and had begun the 42-day countdown to be declared Ebola-free once more. According to the WHO, tests indicated that the flare-up was likely due to contact with a prior Ebola survivor's infected body fluids.<ref name=discharge>{{cite report|url=https://www.who.int/features/2016/ebola-patients-discharge/en/|title=Liberia and Guinea discharge final Ebola patients in latest flare-up and begin 42 days of heightened surveillance|publisher=WHO |access-date=9 June 2016}}</ref> On 9 June, the flare-up was declared over, and the country Ebola-free, due to the passage of the 42-day period;<ref name="true">{{cite news|last1=Farge |first1=Emma |title=Female survivor may be cause of Ebola flare-up in Liberia |url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-liberia-idUSKBN0U02EJ20151217 |work=Reuters|access-date=30 October 2018}}</ref><ref>{{cite report|url=http://www.afro.who.int/en/media-centre/pressreleases/item/8699-who-declares-the-end-of-the-most-recent-ebola-virus-disease-outbreak-in-liberia.html|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160610133447/http://www.afro.who.int/en/media-centre/pressreleases/item/8699-who-declares-the-end-of-the-most-recent-ebola-virus-disease-outbreak-in-liberia.html|url-status=dead|archive-date=10 June 2016|title=WHO declares the end of the most recent Ebola virus disease outbreak in Liberia|publisher=WHO |access-date=9 June 2016}}</ref> Liberia then entered a 90-day period of heightened surveillance, which ended on 7 September 2016. In early July 2016, a trial for males with detectable Ebola [[Ribonucleic acid|RNA]] in semen, started.<ref>{{cite report|title=PREVAIL treatment trial for men with persistent Ebola viral RNA in semen opens in Liberia|url=https://www.nih.gov/news-events/news-releases/prevail-treatment-trial-men-persistent-ebola-viral-rna-semen-opens-liberia|publisher=NIH|access-date=1 August 2016|date=5 July 2016}}</ref>

=== Western African countries with limited local cases ===

==== Senegal ====
In March 2014, the [[Senegal]] Ministry of Interior closed its southern border with Guinea,<ref>{{cite news |url=https://www.bloomberg.com/news/2014-03-29/ebola-virus-death-toll-in-guinea-outbreak-rises-to-70-people.html|title=Ebola Death Toll in Guinea Rises to 70 as Senegal Closes Border |publisher=Bloomberg News|date=29 March 2014 |access-date=31 March 2014 |first=Ougna |last=Camara}}</ref> but on 29 August, the health minister announced the country's first case – a university student from Guinea who was being treated in a [[Dakar]] hospital.<ref name="Ebola virus disease – Senegal">{{cite report|url=http://www.afro.who.int/en/clusters-a-programmes/dpc/epidemic-a-pandemic-alert-and-response/outbreak-news/4265-ebola-virus-disease-senegal.html|title=Ebola virus disease – Senegal|publisher=WHO|date=29 March 2014|access-date=1 September 2014|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140905001908/http://www.afro.who.int/en/clusters-a-programmes/dpc/epidemic-a-pandemic-alert-and-response/outbreak-news/4265-ebola-virus-disease-senegal.html|archive-date=5 September 2014|df=dmy-all}}</ref> The patient was a native of Guinea who had travelled to Dakar, arriving on 20 August. On 23 August, he sought medical care for symptoms including diarrhoea, and vomiting plus signs of fever. He received treatment for malaria but did not improve and left the facility. Still experiencing the same symptoms, on 26 August he was referred to a specialised facility for infectious diseases, and subsequently hospitalised.<ref name="Ebola virus disease – Senegal" />

On 28 August 2014, authorities in Guinea issued an alert informing their medical services and neighbouring countries that a person who had been in close contact with an Ebola-infected patient had escaped their surveillance system. The alert prompted testing for Ebola at the Dakar laboratory, and the positive result launched an investigation, triggering urgent contact tracing.<ref name="Ebola virus disease – Senegal" /> On 10 September, it was reported that the student had recovered but health officials continued to monitor his contacts for 21 days.<ref>{{cite web|title=Guinean Who Brought Ebola To Senegal Recovers From Virus|url=http://www.businessinsider.com/r-guinean-who-brought-ebola-to-senegal-recovers-from-virus-2014-9|website=Business Insider|access-date=20 September 2014}}</ref> No further cases were reported,<ref name="Ebola_Outbreak_total_20_Sept">{{cite report|url=http://apps.who.int/iris/bitstream/10665/134449/1/roadmapupdate22sept14_eng.pdf?ua=1 |format= PDF|title=Ebola Response Roadmap Update, 22 September 2014 |publisher=WHO|date=22 September 2014|access-date=22 September 2014}}</ref> and on 17 October 2014, the WHO officially declared that the outbreak in Senegal had ended.<ref name=WHO20141022 />

The WHO officially commended the Senegalese government, and in particular the [[Macky Sall|President Macky Sall]] and the Minister of Health, [[Awa Coll-Seck|Dr Awa Coll-Seck]], for their response in quickly isolating the patient and tracing and following up 74 contacts, as well as for their public awareness campaign.  This acknowledgement was also extended to MSF and the CDC for their assistance.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://who.int/mediacentre/news/statements/2014/senegal-ends-ebola/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141017214059/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/statements/2014/senegal-ends-ebola/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=17 October 2014|title=WHO congratulates Senegal on ending Ebola transmission|date=17 October 2014|access-date=20 October 2014}}</ref>

==== Nigeria ====
{{Further|Ebola virus disease in Nigeria}}
The first case in Nigeria was a Liberian-American, who flew from Liberia to Nigeria's most populated city of [[Lagos]] on 20 July 2014.  On 6 August 2014, the Nigerian health minister told reporters that one of the nurses that attended to the Liberian had died from the disease. 5 newly confirmed cases were being treated at an isolation ward.<ref name="theguardian1">{{cite news |last=Mark|first=Monica |url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2014/aug/06/ebola-outbreak-nurse-nigeria-dies|title=Ebola Outbreak: Nurse who Treated First Victim in Nigeria Dies|publisher=The Guardian (Nigeria)|date=6 August 2014|access-date=7 August 2014}}</ref>

On 22 September 2014, the Nigerian health ministry announced, "As of today, there is no case of Ebola in Nigeria." According to the WHO, 20&nbsp;cases and 8&nbsp;deaths were confirmed, including the imported case, who also died. 4 of the dead were health workers who had cared for the index case.<ref name="Nigeria in first step towards all-clear on Ebola">{{cite news|url=https://news.yahoo.com/nigeria-first-step-towards-clear-ebola-203222268.html |title=Nigeria in first step towards all-clear on Ebola |publisher=Yahoo News |date=23 September 2014 |access-date=26 September 2014}}</ref>

The WHO's representative in Nigeria officially declared the country Ebola-free on 20 October 2014, after no new active cases were reported in the follow up contacts, stating it was a "spectacular success story".<ref>{{cite report |url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/20-october-2014/en/ |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141021101438/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/20-october-2014/en/ |url-status=dead |archive-date=21 October 2014 |title=Nigeria is now free of Ebola virus transmission |publisher=WHO|date=20 October 2014 |access-date=20 May 2015}}</ref> Nigeria was the first African country to be declared Ebola free.<ref>{{cite report|title=WHO Declares Nigeria Ebola-Free|url=http://www.afro.who.int/en/media-centre/pressreleases/item/7108-who-declares-nigeria-ebola-free.html|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141024235616/http://www.afro.who.int/en/media-centre/pressreleases/item/7108-who-declares-nigeria-ebola-free.html|url-status=dead|archive-date=24 October 2014|publisher=WHO |access-date=22 July 2016}}</ref> This was largely due to the early quarantine efforts of Dr. Ameyo Stella Adadevoh at First Consultants Medical Centre in Lagos.<ref>{{Cite journal|last=Green|first=Andrew|date=2014|title=Remembering health workers who died from Ebola in 2014|journal=The Lancet|language=en|volume=384|issue=9961|pages=2201–2206|doi=10.1016/S0140-6736(14)62417-X|pmid=25625389|s2cid=23124192}}</ref>

==== Mali ====
[[File:Mali- Ebola districts.png|thumb|upright=1.5|Malian regions with Ebola cases (Kayes/Bamako), November 2014]]
{{Further|Ebola virus disease in Mali}}
On 23 October 2014, the first case of Ebola virus disease in [[Mali]] was confirmed in the city of [[Kayes]]—a two-year-old girl who had arrived with a family group from Guinea, and died the next day.<ref name="WHO_sitrep_Mali_10_nov">{{cite report|title=Mali case, Ebola imported from Guinea Ebola situation assessment – 10 November 2014|url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/10-november-2014-mali/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141111151000/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/10-november-2014-mali/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=11 November 2014|publisher=WHO|access-date=11 November 2014}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|title=Ebola Outbreak 2014|url=http://www.breakingnews.com/item/2014/10/24/2-year-old-girl-who-was-malis-1st-ebola-case-has/|work=NBC News |access-date=24 October 2014}}</ref> Her father had worked for the Red Cross in Guinea and also in a private health clinic; he had died earlier in the month, likely from an Ebola infection contracted in the private clinic. It was later established that a number of family members had also died of Ebola. The family had returned to Mali after the father's funeral via public bus and taxi—a journey of more than {{convert|1200|km}}. All contacts were followed for 21&nbsp;days, with no further spread of the disease reported.<ref>{{cite web|title=Mali : Situation de la lutte contre la maladie à virus Ebola au Mali à la date du mercredi 19 novembre 2014|url=http://maliactu.net/mali-situation-de-la-lutte-contre-la-maladie-a-virus-ebola-au-mali-a-la-date-du-mercredi-19-novembre-2014|date=19 November 2014|publisher=maliactu|access-date=26 November 2014|language=fr|archive-date=14 December 2014|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141214092832/http://maliactu.net/mali-situation-de-la-lutte-contre-la-maladie-a-virus-ebola-au-mali-a-la-date-du-mercredi-19-novembre-2014/|url-status=dead}}</ref>

On 12 November 2014, Mali reported deaths from Ebola in an outbreak unconnected with the first case in Kayes. The first probable case was an [[imam]] who had fallen ill on 17 October in Guinea and was transferred to the Pasteur Clinic in Mali's capital city, [[Bamako]], for treatment. He was treated for kidney failure but was not tested for Ebola; he died on 27 October and his body returned to Guinea for burial.<ref>{{cite report|title=Mali confirms its second fatal case of Ebola virus disease|url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/12-november-2014-mali/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141113060100/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/12-november-2014-mali/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=13 November 2014|publisher=WHO|access-date=14 November 2014}}</ref>  A nurse and a doctor who had treated the imam subsequently fell ill with Ebola and died.<ref>{{cite news|title=Update 2 – Doctor who treated source of second Mali Ebola outbreak dies|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/health-ebola-mali-idUSL6N0TA62R20141120|work=Reuters|access-date=21 November 2014|date=21 November 2014}}</ref><ref name="Ebola_case_mali_12_nov">{{cite news|title=Ebola crisis: Third death confirmed in Mali|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-africa-30019895|work=BBC News|date=12 November 2014 |access-date=12 November 2014}}</ref>  The next 3&nbsp;cases were related to the imam as well: a man who had visited the imam while he was in hospital, his wife and his son. On 22 November, the final case related to the imam was reported—a friend of the Pasteur Clinic nurse who had died from the Ebola virus.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-mali-idUSKCN0J60EI20141122|title=Mali records new Ebola case, linked to dead nurse|work=Reuters|access-date=22 November 2014|date=22 November 2014}}</ref> On 12 December, the last case in treatment recovered and was discharged, "so there are no more people sick with Ebola in Mali", according to a Ministry of Health source.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.france24.com/en/20141212-mali-nears-ebola-free-status-last-patient/|title=Africa – Mali nears Ebola-free status as last patient leaves hospital|publisher=France 24|access-date=12 December 2014|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141227162708/http://www.france24.com/en/20141212-mali-nears-ebola-free-status-last-patient/|archive-date=27 December 2014|url-status=dead}}</ref> On 16 December,  Mali released the final 13&nbsp;individuals who were being quarantined<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.news24.com/Africa/News/Mali-ends-quarantines-could-be-Ebola-free-soon-20141216|title=Mali ends quarantines, could be Ebola-free soon|publisher=News24|access-date=17 December 2014|date=2014-12-16}}</ref> and 24&nbsp;days later (18 January 2015) without new cases, the country was declared Ebola-free.<ref name=WHO2015_01_211 />

=== Other countries with limited local cases ===

==== United Kingdom ====
{{Further|Ebola virus disease in the United Kingdom}}
On 29 December 2014, [[Pauline Cafferkey]], a British aid worker who had just returned to [[Glasgow]] from Sierra Leone, was diagnosed with Ebola.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/ebola-in-britain-infected-nurse-pauline-cafferkey-treated-with-survivors-blood-and-experimental-drugs-9951992.html|title=Ebola in the UK: Infected nurse Pauline Cafferkey treated with survivors' blood plasma and experimental drugs|newspaper=The Independent|access-date=1 January 2015|location=London|first=Charlie|last=Cooper|date=31 December 2014}}</ref> She was treated and declared to be free of infection and released from hospital on 24 January 2015.<ref name="Uk_10_March">{{cite report|url=http://www.euro.who.int/en/health-topics/emergencies/pages/news/news/2015/03/united-kingdom-is-declared-free-of-ebola-virus-disease|title=United Kingdom is declared free of Ebola virus disease|publisher=WHO Europe|access-date=11 March 2015}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-30967337|title=Ebola nurse: Pauline Cafferkey 'happy to be alive'|work=BBC News|date=24 January 2015 |access-date= 11 April 2015}}</ref> On 8 October, she was readmitted for complications caused by the virus<ref>{{cite news|title = Ebola nurse Pauline Cafferkey 'in serious condition'|url = https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-scotland-34483584|work= BBC News|access-date = 9 October 2015|date = 2015-10-09}}</ref> and was in "serious" condition, according to a hospital report.  On 14 October, her condition was listed as "critical"<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.cnn.com/2015/10/14/europe/uk-ebola-nurse/|title=UK's first Ebola patient 'critically ill,' in isolation|first1=Greg|last1=Botelho|date=14 October 2015|publisher=CNN|access-date=16 October 2015}}</ref> and 58&nbsp;individuals were being monitored and 25 received an experimental vaccination, being close contacts.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-scotland-34509572|title=Ebola nurse's close contacts identified|work=BBC News|access-date=16 October 2015|date=2015-10-12}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=https://uk.news.yahoo.com/ebola-nurse-58-monitored-deadly-virus-191740171.html#1B1gP9Z|title=Ebola Nurse: 58 Monitored For Deadly Virus|date=13 October 2015|publisher=Yahoo! News UK|access-date=16 October 2015}}</ref>  On 21 October, it was reported that she had been diagnosed with [[meningitis]] caused by the virus persisting in her brain.<ref name="auto8">{{cite news|url=http://www.cbc.ca/news/health/ebola-nurse-uk-1.3281635|title=U.K. Ebola nurse has meningitis caused by persisting Ebola, doctors say|date=21 October 2015|publisher=CBC |access-date=27 October 2015}}</ref> On 12 November, she was released from hospital after making a full recovery.<ref name="davidwilcock">{{cite news|url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/home-news/ebola-nurse-pauline-cafferkey-released-from-london-hospital-after-full-recovery-a6731111.html|title=Ebola nurse Pauline Cafferkey released from London hospital after 'full recovery'|first1=David|last1=Wilcock|date=12 November 2015|newspaper=The Independent}}</ref> However, on 23 February, Ms. Cafferkey was admitted for a third time, "under routine monitoring by the Infectious Diseases Unit&nbsp;... for further investigations", according to a spokesperson.<ref>{{cite web|title=Ebola nurse Pauline Cafferkey flying back to Royal Free Hospital amid ongoing tests|url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/worldnews/ebola/12169799/Ebola-nurse-Pauline-Cafferkey-flying-back-to-Royal-Free-Hospital-amid-ongoing-tests.html |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160223185445/http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/worldnews/ebola/12169799/Ebola-nurse-Pauline-Cafferkey-flying-back-to-Royal-Free-Hospital-amid-ongoing-tests.html |url-status=dead |archive-date=23 February 2016 |publisher=The Telegraph|access-date=28 October 2016|date=2016-02-23 }}</ref><ref>{{cite news|title=Ebola nurse Pauline Cafferkey 'stable' after night in London hospital|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-scotland-35639748|work=BBC News |access-date=28 October 2016|date=24 February 2016}}</ref>

==== Italy ====
On 12 May 2015, it was reported that a nurse, who had been working in Sierra Leone, had been diagnosed with Ebola after returning home to the Italian island of [[Sardinia]]. He was treated at [[Spallanzani Hospital]], the national reference centre for Ebola patients.<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.utsandiego.com/news/2015/may/12/nurse-in-italy-tests-positive-for-ebola-was-in/|title=Nurse in Italy tests positive for Ebola; was in Sierra Leone|newspaper=The San Diego Union-Tribune |access-date=13 May 2015|date=2015-05-12}}</ref>  On 10 June, it was reported that he had recovered and was disease-free and he was released from hospital.<ref>[https://news.yahoo.com/italian-nurse-cured-ebola-130902382.html Italian nurse cured of Ebola], AFP, 10 June 2015</ref>

==== Spain ====
{{Further|Ebola virus disease cases in Spain}}
On 5 August 2014, the [[Brothers Hospitallers of Saint John of God]] confirmed that Brother Miguel Pajares, who had been volunteering in Liberia, had become infected. He was evacuated to Spain and died on 12 August.<ref>{{cite news|title=Muere el religioso español Miguel Pajares a causa del ébola |url=http://www.rtve.es/noticias/20140812/muere-religioso-espanol-miguel-pajares-causa-del-ebola/991420.shtml |publisher=RTVE |date=12 August 2014 |access-date=12 August 2014}}</ref> On 21 September it was announced that Brother Manuel García Viejo, another Spanish citizen who was medical director at the [[St John of God Hospital Sierra Leone]] in [[Lunsar]], had been evacuated to Spain from Sierra Leone after being infected with the virus. His death was announced on 25 September.<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.ibtimes.co.uk/ebola-outbreak-spanish-missionary-manuel-garcia-viejo-dies-deadly-virus-madrid-1467233|title=Ebola Outbreak: Spanish Missionary Manuel Garcia Viejo Dies of Deadly Virus in Madrid|date=25 September 2014|access-date=25 September 2014|newspaper=International Business Times}}</ref>

In October 2014, a nursing assistant, Teresa Romero, who had cared for these patients became unwell and on 6 October tested positive for Ebola,<ref>{{cite news|title=Nurse 'infected with Ebola' in Spain|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-29514920|access-date=6 October 2014|work=BBC News}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.cadenaser.com/sociedad/articulo/enfermera-ingresada-alcorcon-da-positivo-ebola/csrcsrpor/20141006csrcsrsoc_5/Tes|title=La enfermera ingresada en Alcorcón da positivo por ébola – La segunda prueba ha dado positivo y confirma el diagnóstico |publisher=Cadena Ser|date=6 October 2014 |access-date= 11 April 2015}}</ref> making this the first confirmed case of Ebola transmission outside of Africa. On 19 October, it was reported that Romero had recovered, and on 2 December the WHO declared Spain Ebola-free following the passage of 42 days since Teresa Romero was found to be cured.<ref name="Spain_Ebola_outbreak_2_Dec">{{cite news|url=https://abcnews.go.com/Health/wireStory/spain-ebola-free-nurse-recovery-27300284|title=WHO Says Spain Ebola-Free Following Nurse Recovery|date=2 December 2014|access-date=2 December 2014|work=ABC News}}</ref>

==== United States ====
{{Further|Ebola virus cases in the United States}}

On 30 September 2014, the CDC declared its first case of Ebola virus disease.  It disclosed that [[Thomas Eric Duncan]] became infected in Liberia and travelled to [[Dallas]], [[Texas]] on 20 September.  On 26 September, he fell ill and sought medical treatment, but was sent home with antibiotics. He returned to the hospital by ambulance on 28 September and was placed in isolation and tested for Ebola.<ref name="First_Us_case">{{cite report|url=https://www.cdc.gov/media/releases/2014/s930-ebola-confirmed-case.html|title= CDC and Texas Health Department Confirm First Ebola Case Diagnosed in the U.S.|publisher=CDC |date=1 October 2014| access-date=1 October 2014}}</ref><ref>{{cite news| url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/worldnews/ebola/11132475/First-Ebola-victim-in-America-was-sent-home-with-antibiotics.html |archive-url=https://ghostarchive.org/archive/20220112/https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/worldnews/ebola/11132475/First-Ebola-victim-in-America-was-sent-home-with-antibiotics.html |archive-date=12 January 2022 |url-access=subscription |url-status=live| author=Nick Allen| title=First Ebola victim in America was sent home with antibiotics|newspaper=The Telegraph| date=1 October 2014 |access-date= 11 April 2015| location=London}}{{cbignore}}</ref>  He died on 8 October.<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.cnn.com/2014/10/08/health/thomas-eric-duncan-ebola/|title=Thomas Eric Duncan: First Ebola death in U.S.|date=8 October 2014|publisher=CNN|access-date=8 October 2014}}</ref>  Two cases stemmed from Duncan, when two nurses that had treated him tested positive for the virus on 10 and 14 October<ref name="NYT-20141012-MF">{{cite news|last=Fernandez|first=Manny|title=Texas Health Worker Tests Positive for Ebola|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2014/10/13/us/texas-health-worker-tests-positive-for-ebola.html|date=12 October 2014 |newspaper=The New York Times|access-date=12 October 2014}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.foxnews.com/health/2014/10/15/family-identifies-2nd-health-care-worker-infected-with-ebola/|title=Second Ebola-infected nurse ID'd; flew domestic flight day before diagnosis|publisher=Fox News|access-date=15 October 2014|date=15 October 2014}}</ref>  and ended when they were declared Ebola-free on 24 and 22 October, respectively.<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.nbcnews.com/storyline/ebola-virus-outbreak/virus-free-ebola-infected-nurse-nina-pham-go-home-n233171|title=Virus Free: Ebola-Infected Nurse Nina Pham to Go Home|date=24 October 2014 |access-date= 11 April 2015|work=NBC News}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=https://news.yahoo.com/nurse-amber-vinson-free-of-ebola-virus-family-says-001502702.html|title=Nurse Amber Vinson free of Ebola virus, family says|date=23 October 2014|publisher=Yahoo! News|access-date=23 October 2014}}</ref>

A fourth case was identified on 23 October 2014, when [[Ebola virus cases in the United States#Craig Spencer|Craig Spencer]], an American physician who had returned to the United States after treating Ebola patients in Western Africa, tested positive for the virus.<ref name="nbc-spencer 20141023">{{cite web|url=http://www.nbcnews.com/storyline/ebola-virus-outbreak/new-york-doctor-just-back-africa-has-ebola-n232561|title=New York Doctor Just Back From Africa Has Ebola|work=NBC News|date=23 October 2014|access-date=23 October 2014}}</ref>  This case, however, had no relation to those originating from Duncan.  Spencer recovered and was released from hospital on 11 November.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2014/11/11/nyregion/craig-spencer-new-york-doctor-with-ebola-will-leave-bellevue-hospital.html?_r=0|newspaper=The New York Times|date=10 November 2014 |access-date= 11 April 2015|title=Craig Spencer, New York Doctor With Ebola, Will Leave Bellevue Hospital|first=Anemona|last=Hartocollis}}</ref>

=== Countries with medically evacuated cases ===
A number of people who had become infected with Ebola were [[medical evacuation|medically evacuated]] for treatment in [[isolation ward]]s in Europe or the US. They were mostly health workers with one of the NGOs in Western Africa. With the exception of a single isolated case in [[Ebola virus disease in Spain|Spain]], no secondary infections occurred as a result of the medical evacuations.  The [[Ebola virus cases in the United States|US accepted]] four evacuees and three were flown to Germany.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.zeit.de/gesellschaft/2014-10/ebola-patient-in-leipzig-gestorben|title=Infektion: Ebola-Patient in Leipziger Klinik gestorben|date=14 October 2014|publisher=De Zeit|access-date=14 October 2014}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |url=https://uk.news.yahoo.com/ugandan-doctor-cured-ebola-german-hospital-181422896.html#AWJipQ5 |title=Ugandan doctor cured of Ebola in German hospital |publisher=Yahoo! News |date=4 December 2014 |access-date=4 December 2014}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|title=Hamburg clinic dismisses Ebola patient|url=http://www.berliner-zeitung.de/panorama/universitaetsklinikum-eppendorf-klinik-entlaesst-hamburger-ebola-patient,10808334,28645204.html|access-date=4 October 2014|language=de|date=2014-10-04}}</ref>
France,<ref>{{cite web|title=French MSF nurse cured of Ebola|url=http://www.france24.com/en/20141004-french-nurse-cured-ebola-health-minister-msf-touraine/|access-date=4 October 2014|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141004174917/http://www.france24.com/en/20141004-french-nurse-cured-ebola-health-minister-msf-touraine/|archive-date=4 October 2014|url-status=dead}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|title=Ebola: l'agent de l'Unicef soigné à l'hôpital Bégin a quitté la France "guéri"|url=http://www.bfmtv.com/societe/l-agent-de-l-unicef-atteint-d-ebola-a-quitte-la-france-gueri-848374.html|access-date=24 November 2014|language=fr}}</ref> Italy,<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.deccanchronicle.com/150102/world-europe/article/italy-doctor-ebola-has-recovered-reports|title=Italy doctor with Ebola has recovered: reports|newspaper=Deccan Chronicle |date=2 January 2015|access-date=2 January 2015}}</ref> the Netherlands,<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.utsandiego.com/news/2014/dec/23/un-nigerian-peacekeeper-recovers-from-ebola/|title=UN: Nigerian peacekeeper recovers from Ebola|newspaper=The San Diego Union-Tribune|access-date=28 December 2014|date=2014-12-23}}</ref> Norway,<ref>{{cite web|title=– Jeg er utrolig takknemlig|url=http://www.nrk.no/norge/_-jeg-er-utrolig-takknemlig-1.11996836|access-date=20 October 2014|language=no|date=2014-10-20}}</ref><ref name="Yahoo News">{{cite news| url=https://news.yahoo.com/norwegian-ebola-patient-cured-aid-group-150617578.html | title=Norwegian Ebola Patient Cured | publisher=Yahoo! News | date=20 October 2014 | access-date=20 October 2014}}</ref>  Switzerland,<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.cbc.ca/news/health/felix-baez-cuban-doctor-with-ebola-recovers-in-geneva-1.2862976|title=Felix Baez, Cuban doctor with Ebola, recovers in Geneva|publisher=CBC News|date=6 December 2014|access-date=6 December 2014}}</ref> and the United Kingdom [[Ebola virus disease in the United Kingdom|received]] two patients (and five who were exposed).<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/health-31908301|title=BBC News – Ebola: Seventh British health worker sent home|work=BBC News|access-date=19 March 2015|date=2015-03-16}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/health-32088310|title=British medic declared free of Ebola|work=BBC News|date=27 March 2015|access-date=27 March 2015}}</ref>

=== Unrelated outbreak in the Democratic Republic of the Congo ===
{{Further|2014 Democratic Republic of the Congo Ebola virus outbreak}}
In August 2014, the WHO reported an outbreak of Ebola virus in the [[Boende]] District, part of the northern [[Équateur (former province)|Équateur]] province of the Democratic Republic of the Congo (DRC), where 13 people were reported to have died of Ebola-like symptoms.<ref name=DRC_Ebola_case>{{cite report|title=Ebola virus disease – Democratic Republic of Congo E-mail Print Epidemiology and surveillance|url=http://www.afro.who.int/en/clusters-a-programmes/dpc/epidemic-a-pandemic-alert-and-response/outbreak-news/4263-ebola-virus-disease-drc.html|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140830004911/http://www.afro.who.int/en/clusters-a-programmes/dpc/epidemic-a-pandemic-alert-and-response/outbreak-news/4263-ebola-virus-disease-drc.html|url-status=dead|archive-date=30 August 2014|publisher=WHO (WHO)|access-date= 11 April 2015}}</ref> Genetic [[sequencing]] revealed that this outbreak was caused by the Zaire Ebola species, which is native to the DRC; there have been seven previous Ebola outbreaks in the country since 1976. The virology results and epidemiological findings indicated no connection to the epidemic in Western Africa.<ref name=DRC_Ebola_case /><ref name="who.int">{{cite report |url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/2-september-2014/en/ |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140905000320/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/2-september-2014/en/ |url-status=dead |archive-date=5 September 2014 |title=Virological analysis: no link between Ebola outbreaks in west Africa and Democratic Republic of Congo |publisher=WHO |date=2 September 2014 |access-date=7 September 2014}}</ref>

The index case was initially reported to have been a woman from Ikanamongo Village, who became ill with symptoms of Ebola after she had butchered a bush animal.<ref name=DRC_Ebola_case /><ref>{{cite report|url=https://www.cdc.gov/vhf/ebola/outbreaks/history/chronology.html|title=Outbreaks Chronology: Ebola Virus Disease|publisher=CDC |access-date=31 May 2015}}</ref> However, later findings suggested that there may have been several previous cases, and it was reported that pigs in the village may have been infected with Ebola some time before the first human case occurred.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://news.yahoo.com/ebola-sleuths-scour-dr-congo-jungle-source-outbreak-060715032.html|title=Ebola sleuths scour DR Congo jungle for source of outbreak|date=23 October 2014|publisher=Yahoo! News|access-date=23 October 2014}}</ref> The WHO declared the outbreak over on 21 November 2014, after a total of 66 cases and 49 deaths.<ref>{{cite report|title=WHO declares end of Ebola outbreak in the Democratic Republic of Congo|url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/statements/2014/drc-ends-ebola/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141121153335/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/statements/2014/drc-ends-ebola/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=21 November 2014|publisher=WHO|access-date=22 November 2014}}</ref><ref name=WHO20141029>{{cite report |url=http://apps.who.int/iris/bitstream/10665/137376/1/roadmapsitrep_29Oct2014_eng.pdf?ua=1  |format= PDF|title=Ebola response roadmap situation report update |publisher=WHO|date=29 October 2014 |access-date =3 November 2014}}</ref>

== Virology ==
{{See also|Ebola virus disease#Virology|Ebola virus}}
[[File:Ebola virus particles.jpg|thumb|Ebola virus particles using electron micrograph]]
Ebola virus disease is caused by four of six viruses classified in the genus ''[[Ebolavirus]]''. Of the four disease-causing viruses, [[Ebola virus]] (formerly and often still called the Zaire Ebola virus) is dangerous and is the virus responsible for the epidemic in Western Africa.<ref name="Gire 2014">{{cite journal|last1=Gire|first1=S. K.|last2=Goba|first2=A.|last3=Andersen|first3=K. G.|last4=Sealfon|first4=R. S. G.|last5=Park|first5=D. J.|last6=Kanneh|first6=L.|last7=Jalloh|first7=S.|last8=Momoh|first8=M.|last9=Fullah|first9=M.|last10=Dudas|first10=G.|last11=Wohl|first11=S.|last12=Moses|first12=L. M.|last13=Yozwiak|first13=N. L.|last14=Winnicki|first14=S.|last15=Matranga|first15=C. B.|last16=Malboeuf|first16=C. M.|last17=Qu|first17=J.|last18=Gladden|first18=A. D.|last19=Schaffner|first19=S. F.|last20=Yang|first20=X.|last21=Jiang|first21=P.-P.|last22=Nekoui|first22=M.|last23=Colubri|first23=A.|last24=Coomber|first24=M. R.|last25=Fonnie|first25=M.|last26=Moigboi|first26=A.|last27=Gbakie|first27=M.|last28=Kamara|first28=F. K.|last29=Tucker|first29=V.|last30=Konuwa|first30=E.|last31=Saffa|first31=S.|last32=Sellu|first32=J.|last33=Jalloh|first33=A. A.|last34=Kovoma|first34=A.|last35=Koninga|first35=J.|last36=Mustapha|first36=I.|last37=Kargbo|first37=K.|last38=Foday|first38=M.|last39=Yillah|first39=M.|last40=Kanneh|first40=F.|last41=Robert|first41=W.|last42=Massally|first42=J. L. B.|last43=Chapman|first43=S. B.|last44=Bochicchio|first44=J.|last45=Murphy|first45=C.|last46=Nusbaum|first46=C.|last47=Young|first47=S.|last48=Birren|first48=B. W.|last49=Grant|first49=D. S.|last50=Scheiffelin|first50=J. S.|last51=Lander|first51=E. S.|last52=Happi|first52=C.|last53=Gevao|first53=S. M.|last54=Gnirke|first54=A.|last55=Rambaut|first55=A.|last56=Garry|first56=R. F.|last57=Khan|first57=S. H.|last58=Sabeti|first58=P. C.|title=Genomic surveillance elucidates Ebola virus origin and transmission during the 2014 outbreak|journal=Science|date=28 August 2014|volume=345|issue=6202|pages=1369–1372|doi=10.1126/science.1259657|pmid=25214632|pmc=4431643|bibcode=2014Sci...345.1369G}}</ref><ref name=KuhnArch>{{cite journal|last1=Kuhn|first1=Jens H.|last2=Becker|first2=Stephan|last3=Ebihara|first3=Hideki|last4=Geisbert|first4=Thomas W.|last5=Johnson|first5=Karl M.|last6=Kawaoka|first6=Yoshihiro|last7=Lipkin|first7=W. Ian|last8=Negredo|first8=Ana I.|last9=Netesov|first9=Sergey V.|last10=Nichol|first10=Stuart T.|last11=Palacios|first11=Gustavo|last12=Peters|first12=Clarence J.|last13=Tenorio|first13=Antonio|last14=Volchkov|first14=Viktor E.|last15=Jahrling|first15=Peter B.|title=Proposal for a revised taxonomy of the family Filoviridae: classification, names of taxa and viruses, and virus abbreviations|journal=Archives of Virology|date=30 October 2010|volume=155|issue=12|pages=2083–2103|doi=10.1007/s00705-010-0814-x|pmid=21046175|pmc=3074192}}</ref>
Since the discovery of the viruses in 1976, when outbreaks occurred in South Sudan (then Sudan) and Democratic Republic of the Congo (then Zaire), Ebola virus disease had been confined to areas in Middle Africa, where it is native. With the current outbreak, it was initially thought that a new species native to Guinea might be the cause, rather than being imported from Middle to Western Africa.<ref name="Baize-2014" /> However, further studies have shown that the outbreak was likely caused by an Ebola virus lineage that spread from Middle Africa via an animal host within the last decade, with the first viral transfer to humans in Guinea.<ref name="Gire 2014" /><ref name="EurekAlert!">{{cite journal|last1=Schoepp|first1=Randal J.|last2=Rossi|first2=Cynthia A.|last3=Khan|first3=Sheik H.|last4=Goba|first4=Augustine|last5=Fair|first5=Joseph N.|title=Undiagnosed Acute Viral Febrile Illnesses, Sierra Leone|journal=Emerging Infectious Diseases|volume=20|issue=7|pages=1176–1182|doi=10.3201/eid2007.131265|pmid=24959946|pmc=4073864|year=2014}}</ref>

In a study done by [[Tulane University]], the [[Broad Institute]] and [[Harvard University]], in partnership with the [[Sierra Leone Ministry of Health and Sanitation]], researchers provided information about the origin and transmission of the Ebola virus that set the Western African outbreak apart from previous ones, including 341 genetic changes in the [[virion]].<ref name="Gire 2014" /> Five members of the research team became ill and died from Ebola before the study was published in August 2014.<ref name="Gire 2014" />
[[File:Ebola Virus Particles (8).jpg|thumb|Ebola virus particles]]
In a report released in August 2014,  researchers tracked the spread of Ebola in Sierra Leone from the group first infected—13 women who had attended the funeral of the traditional healer, where they contracted the disease—giving them a unique opportunity to track how the virus had changed.  This provided "the first time that the real evolution of the Ebola virus [could] be observed in humans." The research showed that the outbreak in Sierra Leone was sparked by at least two distinct lineages introduced from Guinea at about the same time. It is not clear whether the traditional healer was infected with both variants, or if perhaps one of the women attending the funeral was independently infected. As the Sierra Leone epidemic progressed, one virus lineage disappeared from patient samples, while a third one appeared.<ref>{{Cite journal | title=Genomes reveal start of Ebola outbreak | journal=Science | volume=345 | issue=6200 | pages=989–990 | date=29 August 2014 | last1=Vogel|first1= Gretchen| bibcode=2014Sci...345..989V | pmid=25170128 | doi=10.1126/science.345.6200.989 | doi-access=free }}</ref><ref>{{cite report|url=https://www.gov.uk/government/news/phe-study-finds-ebola-virus-mutated-slower-than-first-thought|title=PHE study finds Ebola virus mutated slower than first thought|author=Public Health England|publisher=Public Health England|access-date=15 July 2015}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.dddmag.com/news/2015/06/scientists-release-ebola-sequencing-data-global-research-community-online|title=Scientists Release Ebola Sequencing Data to Global Research Community Online|publisher=Drug Discovery & Development|access-date=7 June 2015|date=2015-06-03}}</ref><ref>{{cite journal|last1=Simon-Loriere|first1=Etienne|last2=Faye|first2=Ousmane|last3=Faye|first3=Oumar|last4=Koivogui|first4=Lamine|last5=Magassouba|first5=Nfaly|last6=Keita|first6=Sakoba|last7=Thiberge|first7=Jean-Michel|last8=Diancourt|first8=Laure|last9=Bouchier|first9=Christiane|last10=Vandenbogaert|first10=Matthias|last11=Caro|first11=Valérie|last12=Fall|first12=Gamou|last13=Buchmann|first13=Jan P.|last14=Matranga|first14=Christan B.|last15=Sabeti|first15=Pardis C.|last16=Manuguerra|first16=Jean-Claude|last17=Holmes|first17=Edward C.|last18=Sall|first18=Amadou A.|title=Distinct lineages of Ebola virus in Guinea during the 2014 West African epidemic|journal=Nature|date=6 August 2015|volume=524|issue=7563|pages=102–104|doi=10.1038/nature14612|pmid=26106863|bibcode=2015Natur.524..102S|doi-access=free}}</ref>

In January 2015, the media stated researchers in Guinea had reported mutations in the virus samples that they were looking at.  According to them, "we've now seen several cases that don't have any symptoms at all, asymptomatic cases. These people may be the people who can spread the virus better, but we still don't know that yet. A virus can change itself to [become] less deadly, but more contagious and that's something we are afraid of."<ref>{{cite magazine|url=http://time.com/3687240/ebola-disease-health-vaccine-guinea/|title=Ebola Virus has Mutated, Scientists Say|author=Kevin McSpadden|magazine=Time|access-date=31 January 2015|date=29 January 2015}}</ref> A 2015 study suggested that accelerating the rate of mutation of the Ebola virus could make the virus less capable of infecting humans. In this animal study, the virus became practically non-viable, consequently increasing survival.<ref>{{cite journal |date=16 December 2015 |title=Determination of the Spontaneous Mutation Frequency of Ebola virus and Exploitation of this Therapeutically|journal=Journal of Virology |volume=90 |issue=5 |pages=2345–2355 |doi=10.1128/JVI.02701-15|pmid=26676781 |pmc=4810732|last1=Alfson |first1=K. J. |last2=Worwa |first2=G. |last3=Carrion Jr |first3=R. |last4=Griffiths |first4=A. }}</ref>

== Transmission ==
{{See also|Ebola virus disease#Transmission}}

=== Animal to human transmission ===
[[File:EbolaCycle.png|thumb|upright=1.5|The life cycles of the Ebolavirus]]
The initial infection is believed to occur after an Ebola virus is transmitted to a human by contact with an infected animal's body fluids. Evidence strongly implicates bats as the reservoir hosts for ebolaviruses (however, despite considerable research, infectious ebolaviruses have never been recovered from bats).<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Griensven |first1=Johan van |last2=Günther |first2=Stephan |last3=Clerck |first3=Hilde de |last4=McElroy |first4=Anita K. |last5=Malvy |first5=Denis |title=Ebola virus disease |journal=The Lancet |date=2 March 2019 |volume=393 |issue=10174 |pages=936–948 |doi=10.1016/S0140-6736(18)33132-5 |pmid=30777297 |doi-access=free }}</ref><ref>{{Cite journal|last1=Ng|first1=Melinda|last2=Ndungo|first2=Esther|last3=Kaczmarek|first3=Maria E|last4=Herbert|first4=Andrew S|last5=Binger|first5=Tabea|last6=Kuehne|first6=Ana I|last7=Jangra|first7=Rohit K|last8=Hawkins|first8=John A|last9=Gifford|first9=Robert J|date=23 December 2015|title=Filovirus receptor NPC1 contributes to species-specific patterns of ebolavirus susceptibility in bats|journal=eLife|volume=4|page=e11785|doi=10.7554/eLife.11785|pmid=26698106|pmc=4709267}}</ref>
Bats drop partially eaten fruit and pulp, then land mammals such as [[gorilla]]s and [[duiker]]s feed on this fallen fruit. This chain of events forms a possible indirect means of transmission from the natural host to animal populations.<ref>{{Cite book |last1=Gonzalez |first1=J. P.|last2= Pourrut|first2= X.|last3= Leroy|first3= E. |volume=315 |pages=363–387 |year=2007 |pmid=17848072 |doi=10.1007/978-3-540-70962-6_15 |isbn=978-3-540-70961-9 |series=Current Topics in Microbiology and Immunology |title=Wildlife and Emerging Zoonotic Diseases: The Biology, Circumstances and Consequences of Cross-Species Transmission |chapter=Ebolavirus and Other Filoviruses|pmc=7121322|editor1-last=Childs|editor1-first=J. E.|editor2-last=Mackenzie|editor2-first=J. S.|editor3-last=Richt|editor3-first=J. A. }}</ref> As primates in the area were not found to be infected and [[fruit bats]] do not live near the location of the initial [[Zoonosis|zoonotic transmission]] event in Meliandou, Guinea, it is suspected that the index case occurred after a child had contact with an [[Microbat|insectivorous bat]] from a colony of [[Angolan free-tailed bat]]s near the village.<ref name=EMBOMM123014>{{cite journal |last1=Mari Saez |first1=A. |last2=Weiss |first2=S. |last3=Nowak |first3=K. |last4=Lapeyre |first4=V. |last5=Zimmermann |first5=F. |last6=Dux |first6=A. |last7=Kuhl |first7=H. S. |last8=Kaba |first8=M. |last9=Regnaut |first9=S. |last10=Merkel |first10=K. |last11=Sachse |first11=A. |last12=Thiesen |first12=U. |last13=Villanyi |first13=L. |last14=Boesch |first14=C. |last15=Dabrowski |first15=P. W. |last16=Radonic |first16=A. |last17=Nitsche |first17=A. |last18=Leendertz |first18=S. A. J. |last19=Petterson |first19=S. |last20=Becker |first20=S. |last21=Krahling |first21=V. |last22=Couacy-Hymann |first22=E. |last23=Akoua-Koffi |first23=C. |last24=Weber |first24=N. |last25=Schaade |first25=L. |last26=Fahr |first26=J. |last27=Borchert |first27=M. |last28=Gogarten |first28=J. F. |last29=Calvignac-Spencer |first29=S. |last30=Leendertz |first30=F. H. |title=Investigating the zoonotic origin of the West African Ebola epidemic |journal=EMBO Molecular Medicine |date=30 December 2014 |volume=7 |issue=1 |pages=17–23 |doi=10.15252/emmm.201404792|pmid=25550396|pmc=4309665|quote=The severe Ebola virus disease epidemic occurring in West Africa likely stems from a single zoonotic transmission event involving a 2‐year‐old boy in Meliandou, Guinea, who might have been infected by hunting or playing with insectivorous free‐tailed bats living in a nearby hollow tree}}</ref>

On 12 January, the journal ''Nature'' reported that the virus emergence could be found by studying how bush-meat hunters interacted with the ecosystem.<ref>{{cite journal|title=Hunt for Ebola's wild hideout takes off as epidemic wanes|journal=Nature|volume=529|issue=7585|pages=138–139|publisher=Nature News & Comment|doi=10.1038/529138a|pmid=26762436|year=2016|last1=Callaway|first1=Ewen|bibcode=2016Natur.529..138C|doi-access=free}}</ref> The continent of Africa has experienced deforestation in several areas or regions; this may contribute to recent outbreaks, including this epidemic, as initial cases have been in the proximity of deforested lands where fruit-eating bats natural habitat may be affected, though 100% evidence does not as yet exist.<ref name=defor/><ref name=defor2/>

=== Human to human transmission ===

Prior to this outbreak, it was believed that human-to-human transmission occurred only via direct contact with blood or bodily fluids from an infected person who is showing symptoms of infection, by contact with the body of a person who had died of Ebola, or by contact with objects recently contaminated with the body fluids of an actively ill infected person.<ref name=CDCPress2014>{{cite report|title=CDC Telebriefing on Ebola outbreak in West Africa|url=https://www.cdc.gov/media/releases/2014/t0728-ebola.html|publisher=CDC|access-date=3 August 2014|date=28 July 2014}}</ref><ref name=Ost2015>{{cite journal|last1=Osterholm|first1=MT|last2=Moore|first2=KA|last3=Kelley|first3=NS|last4=Brosseau|first4=LM|last5=Wong|first5=G|last6=Murphy|first6=FA|last7=Peters|first7=CJ|last8=LeDuc|first8=JW|last9=Russell|first9=PK|last10=Van Herp|first10=M|last11=Kapetshi|first11=J|last12=Muyembe|first12=JJ|last13=Ilunga|first13=BK|last14=Strong|first14=JE|last15=Grolla|first15=A|last16=Wolz|first16=A|last17=Kargbo|first17=B|last18=Kargbo|first18=DK|last19=Formenty|first19=P|last20=Sanders|first20=DA|author21-link=Gary Kobinger|last21=Kobinger|first21=GP|title=Transmission of Ebola viruses: what we know and what we do not know|journal=mBio|date=19 February 2015|volume=6|issue=2|page=e00137|pmid=25698835|doi=10.1128/mBio.00137-15|pmc=4358015}}</ref>
It is now known that the Ebola virus can be transmitted sexually. Over time, studies have suggested that the virus can persist in [[seminal fluid]], with a study released in September 2016 suggesting that the virus may survive more than 530&nbsp;days after infection.<ref name=BDiallo/> EBOV RNA in semen is not the same situation as perseverance of EBOV in semen, however the "clinical significance of low levels of virus RNA in convalescent" individuals who are healthy is unknown.<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Broadhurst |first1=M. Jana |last2=Brooks |first2=Tim J. G. |last3=Pollock |first3=Nira R. |title=Diagnosis of Ebola Virus Disease: Past, Present, and Future |journal=Clinical Microbiology Reviews |date=October 2016 |volume=29 |issue=4 |pages=773–793 |doi=10.1128/CMR.00003-16 |pmid=27413095 |pmc=5010747}}</ref><ref>{{cite book |editor1-last=Mühlberger |editor1-first=Elke |editor2-last=Hensley |editor2-first=Lisa L. |editor3-last=Towner |editor3-first=Jonathan S. |title=Marburg- and Ebolaviruses: From Ecosystems to Molecules |volume=411 |date=2017 |publisher=Springer |isbn=978-3-319-68948-7 |page=79|chapter=West Africa 2013 Ebola: From Virus Outbreak to Humanitarian Crisis|last1=Bausch|first1=Daniel G.|doi=10.1007/82_2017_69 |pmid=29071473 |chapter-url=https://books.google.com/books?id=gkFEDwAAQBAJ&q=EBOV+RNA+in+semen&pg=PA79 |access-date=14 January 2019 |series=Current Topics in Microbiology and Immunology }}</ref>

In September 2014, the WHO had reported: "No formal evidence exists of sexual transmission, but sexual transmission from convalescent patients cannot be ruled out. There is evidence that live Ebola virus can be isolated in seminal fluids of convalescent men for 82 days after onset of symptoms. Evidence is not available yet beyond 82 days."<ref name="Ebola media centre fact sheets">{{cite report |url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/factsheets/fs103/en/ |title=Ebola media centre fact sheets |publisher=WHO |access-date=21 September 2014}}</ref>  In April 2015, following a report that the RNA virus had been detected in a semen sample six months after a man's recovery, the WHO issued a statement: "For greater security and prevention of other sexually transmitted infections, Ebola survivors should consider correct and consistent use of condoms for all sexual acts beyond three months until more information is available."<ref>{{cite report|title=Interim advice on the sexual transmission of the Ebola virus disease|url=https://www.who.int/reproductivehealth/topics/rtis/ebola-virus-semen/en/|publisher=WHO |access-date=28 October 2016}}</ref><ref>{{cite journal|last1=Deen|first1=Gibrilla F.|last2=Knust|first2=Barbara|last3=Broutet|first3=Nathalie|last4=Sesay|first4=Foday R.|last5=Formenty|first5=Pierre|last6=Ross|first6=Christine|last7=Thorson|first7=Anna E.|last8=Massaquoi|first8=Thomas A.|last9=Marrinan|first9=Jaclyn E.|last10=Ervin|first10=Elizabeth|last11=Jambai|first11=Amara|last12=McDonald|first12=Suzanna L.R.|last13=Bernstein|first13=Kyle|last14=Wurie|first14=Alie H.|last15=Dumbuya|first15=Marion S.|last16=Abad|first16=Neetu|last17=Idriss|first17=Baimba|last18=Wi|first18=Teodora|last19=Bennett|first19=Sarah D.|last20=Davies|first20=Tina|last21=Ebrahim|first21=Faiqa K.|last22=Meites|first22=Elissa|last23=Naidoo|first23=Dhamari|last24=Smith|first24=Samuel|last25=Banerjee|first25=Anshu|last26=Erickson|first26=Bobbie Rae|last27=Brault|first27=Aaron|last28=Durski|first28=Kara N.|last29=Winter|first29=Jorn|last30=Sealy|first30=Tara|last31=Nichol|first31=Stuart T.|last32=Lamunu|first32=Margaret|last33=Ströher|first33=Ute|last34=Morgan|first34=Oliver|last35=Sahr|first35=Foday|title=Ebola RNA Persistence in Semen of Ebola Virus Disease Survivors — Preliminary Report|journal=New England Journal of Medicine|date=14 October 2015|volume=377|issue=15|pages=1428–1437|doi=10.1056/NEJMoa1511410|pmid=26465681|pmc=5798881}}</ref>

The WHO based their new recommendations on a March 2015 case, in which a Liberian woman who had no contact with the disease other than having had unprotected sex with a man who had had the disease in October 2014, was diagnosed with Ebola. While no evidence of the virus was found in his blood, his semen revealed Ebola virus RNA closely matching the variant that infected the woman. However, "doctors don't know if there was any fully formed (and therefore infectious) virus in the guy's semen."  It is known that testes are protected from the body's immune system to protect the developing [[sperm]], and it is thought that this same protection may allow the virus to survive in the testes for an unknown time.<ref>{{cite magazine|url=http://www.popsci.com/why-ebola-lives-so-long-survivors-testicles|title=Why Testicles Are The Perfect Hiding Spot For Ebola|magazine=Popular Science|access-date=7 May 2015}}</ref>

On 14 September 2015, the body of a girl who had died in Sierra Leone tested positive for Ebola<ref name="auto6" />  and it was suspected that she may have contracted the disease from the semen of an Ebola survivor who was discharged in March 2015.<ref name="auto7" /> According to some news reports, a new study to be published in the ''New England Journal of Medicine'' indicated that the RNA virus could remain in the semen of survivors for up to six months,<ref>{{cite news|url=http://uk.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-semen-idUKKCN0R922G20150909|title=Sex and masturbation may hamper Ebola eradication efforts|work=Reuters|access-date=14 September 2015|date=9 September 2015 }}</ref><ref>{{cite journal |last1=Deen |first1=Gibrilla F. |last2=Broutet |first2=Nathalie |last3=Xu |first3=Wenbo |last4=Knust |first4=Barbara |last5=Sesay |first5=Foday R. |last6=McDonald |first6=Suzanna L.R. |last7=Ervin |first7=Elizabeth |last8=Marrinan |first8=Jaclyn E. |last9=Gaillard |first9=Philippe |last10=Habib |first10=Ndema |last11=Liu |first11=Hongtu |last12=Liu |first12=William |last13=Thorson |first13=Anna E. |last14=Yamba |first14=Francis |last15=Massaquoi |first15=Thomas A. |last16=James |first16=Faustin |last17=Ariyarajah |first17=Archchun |last18=Ross |first18=Christine |last19=Bernstein |first19=Kyle |last20=Coursier |first20=Antoine |last21=Klena |first21=John |last22=Carino |first22=Marylin |last23=Wurie |first23=Alie H. |last24=Zhang |first24=Yong |last25=Dumbuya |first25=Marion S. |last26=Abad |first26=Neetu |last27=Idriss |first27=Baimba |last28=Wi |first28=Teodora |last29=Bennett |first29=Sarah D. |last30=Davies |first30=Tina |last31=Ebrahim |first31=Faiqa K. |last32=Meites |first32=Elissa |last33=Naidoo |first33=Dhamari |last34=Smith |first34=Samuel J. |last35=Ongpin |first35=Patricia |last36=Malik |first36=Tasneem |last37=Banerjee |first37=Anshu |last38=Erickson |first38=Bobbie R. |last39=Liu |first39=Yongjian |last40=Liu |first40=Yang |last41=Xu |first41=Ke |last42=Brault |first42=Aaron |last43=Durski |first43=Kara N. |last44=Winter |first44=Jörn |last45=Sealy |first45=Tara |last46=Nichol |first46=Stuart T. |last47=Lamunu |first47=Margaret |last48=Bangura |first48=James |last49=Landoulsi |first49=Sihem |last50=Jambai |first50=Amara |last51=Morgan |first51=Oliver |last52=Wu |first52=Guizhen |last53=Liang |first53=Mifang |last54=Su |first54=Qiudong |last55=Lan |first55=Yu |last56=Hao |first56=Yanzhe |last57=Formenty |first57=Pierre |last58=Ströher |first58=Ute |last59=Sahr |first59=Foday |title=Ebola RNA Persistence in Semen of Ebola Virus Disease Survivors — Final Report |journal=New England Journal of Medicine |date=12 October 2017 |volume=377 |issue=15 |pages=1428–1437 |doi=10.1056/NEJMoa1511410 |pmid=26465681 |pmc=5798881 }}</ref> and according to other researchers, the RNA virus could continue in semen for 82 days and maybe longer. Furthermore, Ebola RNA had been found up to 284 days post-onset of viral symptoms.<ref>{{Cite journal|last1=Broutet|first1=Nathalie|last2=Lofthouse|first2=Clare|last3=Formenty|first3=Pierre|last4=Thorson|first4=Anna|date=2016|title=Systematic review of the literature on viral persistence and sexual transmission from recovered Ebola survivors: evidence and recommendations|journal=BMJ Open|volume=6|issue=1|page=e008859|doi=10.1136/bmjopen-2015-008859|pmid=26743699|pmc=4716240}}</ref>

=== Containment difficulties ===

One of the primary reasons for the spread of the disease is the low-quality, functioning health systems in the parts of Africa where the disease occurs.<ref>{{cite web|first1=Tiaji|last1=Salaam-Blyther|title=The 2014 Ebola Outbreak: International and U.S. Responses|url=https://fas.org/sgp/crs/row/R43697.pdf|access-date=9 September 2014|date=26 August 2014}}</ref> The risk of transmission is increased among those caring for people infected. Recommended measures when caring for those who are infected include [[Isolation (health care)|medical isolation]] via the proper use of boots, gowns, gloves, masks and goggles, and [[disinfection|sterilizing all equipment and surfaces]].<ref>{{cite report|title=Prevention of Ebola Hemorrhagic Fever |publisher=CDC|url=https://www.cdc.gov/vhf/ebola/prevention/index.html |access-date=28 October 2016}}</ref>

One of the biggest dangers of infection faced by medical staff requires their learning how to properly suit up and remove personal protective equipment.
Full training for wearing protective body clothing can take 10 to 14 days.<ref name="www.telegraph.co.uk Ebola-medics-better-trained-in-Sierra-Leone-than-Spain">[https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/worldnews/ebola/11155840/Ebola-medics-better-trained-in-Sierra-Leone-than-Spain.html Ebola medics 'better trained in Sierra Leone than Spain'] ''The Telegraph'', by Fiona Govan, 11 October 2014</ref>  Even with proper isolation equipment available, working conditions such as lack of running water, climate control, and flooring have made direct care difficult. Two American health workers who contracted the disease and later recovered said that to the best of their knowledge, their team of workers had been following "to the letter all of the protocols for safety that were developed by the [CDC] and WHO", including a full body coverall, several layers of gloves, and face protection including goggles. One of the two, a physician, had worked with patients, but the other was assisting workers to get in and out of their protective gear while wearing protective gear herself.<ref name="nbcnews.com">{{cite news|url=http://www.nbcnews.com/storyline/ebola-virus-outbreak/two-americans-stricken-deadly-ebola-virus-liberia-n166281 |title=Two Americans Stricken With Deadly Ebola Virus in Liberia |work=NBC News |date=28 July 2014 |access-date=2 August 2014}}</ref>

Difficulties in attempting to halt transmission have also included the multiple disease outbreaks across country borders.<ref name="Control" /> [[Peter Piot|Dr Peter Piot]], the scientist who co-discovered the Ebola virus, stated that the outbreak was not following its usual linear patterns as mapped out in earlier outbreaks—this time the virus was "hopping" all over the Western African epidemic region.<ref name=Guinea_Ebola_Sep_Update /> Furthermore, most past epidemics had occurred in remote regions, but this outbreak spread to large urban areas, which had increased the number of contacts an infected person might have and made transmission harder to track and break.<ref name="WHO20140825">{{cite report |url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/25-august-2014/en/ |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140827093127/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/25-august-2014/en/ |url-status=dead |archive-date=27 August 2014 |title=Unprecedented number of medical staff infected with Ebola |publisher=WHO |date=25 August 2014 |access-date=27 August 2014}}</ref> On 9 December, a study indicated that a single individual introduced the virus into Liberia, causing the most cases of the disease in that country.<ref>{{cite journal|url= |title=Evolution and Spread of Ebola Virus in Liberia, 2014–2015|journal=Cell Host & Microbe|doi=10.1016/j.chom.2015.11.008|pmid=26651942|pmc=4711363|volume=18|issue=6|pages=659–669|year=2015|last1=Ladner|first1=Jason T|last2=Wiley|first2=Michael R|last3=Mate|first3=Suzanne|last4=Dudas|first4=Gytis|last5=Prieto|first5=Karla|last6=Lovett|first6=Sean|last7=Nagle|first7=Elyse R|last8=Beitzel|first8=Brett|last9=Gilbert|first9=Merle L|last10=Fakoli|first10=Lawrence|last11=Diclaro|first11=Joseph W|last12=Schoepp|first12=Randal J|last13=Fair|first13=Joseph|last14=Kuhn|first14=Jens H|last15=Hensley|first15=Lisa E|last16=Park|first16=Daniel J|last17=Sabeti|first17=Pardis C|last18=Rambaut|first18=Andrew|last19=Sanchez-Lockhart|first19=Mariano|last20=Bolay|first20=Fatorma K|last21=Kugelman|first21=Jeffrey R|last22=Palacios|first22=Gustavo}}</ref>

== Containment and control ==
{{Main|Prevention of viral hemorrhagic fever}}
{{See also|Ebola virus disease#Prevention}}
[[File:PPE Training (2).jpg|thumb|Nigerian doctors training on PPE by WHO]]
In August 2014, the WHO published a road map of the steps required to bring the epidemic under control and to prevent further transmission of the disease within Western Africa; the coordinated [[Responses to the Ebola virus epidemic in West Africa#WHO|international response]] worked towards realising this plan.<ref name="res">{{cite report|title=Ebola response Roadmap|url=http://apps.who.int/iris/bitstream/10665/131596/1/EbolaResponseRoadmap.pdf| publisher=WHO|access-date=21 June 2016}}</ref>

=== Surveillance and contact tracing ===
[[Contact tracing]] is an essential method of preventing the spread of the disease, this requires effective [[Epidemiological surveillance|community surveillance]] so that a possible case of Ebola can be registered and accurately diagnosed as soon as possible, and subsequently finding everyone who has had close contact with the case and tracking them for 21 days. However, this requires careful record-keeping by properly trained and equipped staff.<ref>{{cite report |title=Contact tracing |url=https://www.who.int/features/qa/contact-tracing/en/ |publisher=WHO |access-date=27 January 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|title=The reason Ebola isn't being stopped|url=http://edition.cnn.com/2014/09/11/health/ebola-contact-tracing/|publisher=CNN|access-date=16 September 2014}}</ref> WHO Assistant Director-General for Global Health Security, Keiji Fukuda, said on 3 September 2014, "We don't have enough health workers, doctors, nurses, drivers, and contact tracers to handle the increasing number of cases."<ref name="WHO 2014-9-3 Ebola response needs">{{cite report|title=UN senior leaders outline needs for global Ebola response|url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/releases/2014/ebola-response-needs/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140907161412/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/releases/2014/ebola-response-needs/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=7 September 2014|publisher=WHO|access-date=16 September 2014}}</ref> There was a massive effort to train volunteers and health workers, sponsored by [[United States Agency for International Development]] (USAID).<ref>{{cite report|title=Fact sheet: U.S. Response to the Ebola Epidemic in West Africa|url=https://obamawhitehouse.archives.gov/the-press-office/2014/09/16/fact-sheet-us-response-ebola-epidemic-west-africa|via=[[NARA|National Archives]]|work=[[whitehouse.gov]]|access-date=2 November 2014}}</ref> According to WHO reports, 25,926 contacts from Guinea, 35,183 from Liberia and 104,454 from Sierra Leone were listed and traced as of 23 November 2014.<ref name=WHO2014_11_23 /> According to one study, it is important to have a public awareness campaign to inform the affected community about the importance of contact tracing, so that true information can be obtained from the community.<ref>{{cite journal|title=Learning from the challenges of Ebola Virus Disease contact tracers in Sierra Leone, February, 2015|pmc=4695526|pmid=26740849|doi=10.11694/pamj.supp.2015.22.1.6537|volume=22|issue=Suppl 1|year=2015|page=21| last1 = Ilesanmi | first1 = OS | journal=Pan Afr Med J|doi-broken-date=28 February 2022}}</ref>

=== Community awareness ===
{{See also|Cultural effects of the Ebola crisis}}
To reduce the spread, the WHO recommended raising community awareness of the risk factors for Ebola infection and the protective measures individuals can take.<ref name=WHOFS103>{{cite report|title= Ebola virus disease Fact sheet N°103 |url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/factsheets/fs103/en/|publisher=WHO|access-date=6 September 2014 }}</ref> These include avoiding contact with infected people and regular [[hand washing]] using soap and water.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://plan-international.org/about-plan/resources/news/ebola-outbreak-5-tips-to-avoid-the-deadly-disease/|title=Ebola – 5 tips to avoid the deadly disease |publisher= Plan International|date=6 September 2014 |access-date= 11 April 2015}}</ref> A condition of extreme poverty exists in many of the areas that experienced a high incidence of infections. According to the director of the NGO [[Plan (aid organisation)|Plan International]] in Guinea, "The poor [[living conditions]] and lack of [[water supply|water]] and [[sanitation]] in most districts of Conakry pose a serious risk that the epidemic escalates into a crisis.
