From the beginning of the outbreak, there existed considerable difficulty in getting reliable estimates—both of the number of people affected and of its geographical extent.<ref name=WHO22August>{{cite report|title=Why the Ebola outbreak has been underestimated|url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/22-august-2014/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140823063553/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/news/ebola/22-august-2014/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=23 August 2014|publisher=WHO|access-date=9 October 2014}}</ref> The three most affected countries—Guinea, Liberia and Sierra Leone—are among the poorest in the world, with extremely low levels of literacy, few hospitals or doctors, low-quality physical infrastructure, and weakly functioning government institutions.<ref>{{cite web|title=West & Central Africa – Information by Country|url=http://www.unicef.org/infobycountry/centralafrica.html|publisher=UNICEF|access-date=9 October 2014|archive-date=7 October 2014|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141007160127/http://www.unicef.org/infobycountry/centralafrica.html|url-status=dead}}</ref> One study yielded results of the spatio-temporal evolution of the viral outbreak. With the use of [[heat maps]], it was determined that the outbreak did not uniformly unfold over the affected community areas. Growth in the regions of Guinea, Liberia and Sierra Leone was very different over time, indicating that monitoring the outbreak at district level was important. Visual inspection of incidence curves alone could not render the needed results or data; growth rates with a two-dimensional heat map were used. Finally, the study showed that accurate predictions of growth were improbable, coupled with knowledge about the disease that was not fully adequate at the time (as there were now cases of sexual transmission).<ref>{{cite journal|last1=Santermans|first1=Eva|last2=Robesyn|first2=Emmanuel|last3=Ganyani|first3=Tapiwa|last4=Sudre|first4=Bertrand|last5=Faes|first5=Christel|last6=Quinten|first6=Chantal|last7=Bortel|first7=Wim Van|last8=Haber|first8=Tom|last9=Kovac|first9=Thomas|last10=Reeth|first10=Frank Van|last11=Testa|first11=Marco|last12=Hens|first12=Niel|last13=Plachouras|first13=Diamantis|title=Spatiotemporal Evolution of Ebola Virus Disease at Sub-National Level during the 2014 West Africa Epidemic: Model Scrutiny and Data Meagreness|journal=PLOS ONE|date=15 January 2016|volume=11|issue=1|pages=e0147172|doi=10.1371/journal.pone.0147172|issn=1932-6203|pmid=26771513|pmc=4714854|bibcode=2016PLoSO..1147172S|doi-access=free}}</ref>

=== Statistical measures ===
Calculating the ''[[case fatality rate]]'' (CFR) accurately is difficult in an ongoing epidemic due to differences in testing policies, the inclusion of probable and suspected cases, and the inclusion of new cases that have not run their course. In August 2014, the WHO made an initial CFR estimate of 53%, though this included suspected cases.<ref name=epi-fatal-rate>{{cite news|author=Rambaut, Andrew |title=Case Fatality Rate for ebolavirus |url=http://epidemic.bio.ed.ac.uk/ebolavirus_fatality_rate |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140829075833/http://epidemic.bio.ed.ac.uk/ebolavirus_fatality_rate |url-status=dead |archive-date=29 August 2014 |date=7 August 2014 |publisher=Epidemic |access-date=16 September 2014 }}</ref><ref name="WHO_31Aug">{{cite report|url=http://who.int/csr/don/2014_09_04_ebola/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140904215919/http://who.int/csr/don/2014_09_04_ebola/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=4 September 2014|title=Ebola Response Roadmap Situation Report 2, 5 September 2014|publisher=WHO|access-date=18 September 2014}}</ref> In September and December 2014, the WHO released revised and more accurate CFR figures of 70.8% and 71% respectively, using data from patients with definitive clinical outcomes.<ref name=":1" /><ref name="cfrebolavirus" /><ref name=WHO2014_12_28 /> The CFR among hospitalised patients, based on the three intense-transmission countries, was between 57% and 59% in January 2015.<ref name="WHOreport" />

''Mortality'' is measured by number of deaths in a population per the proportion of the population per unit of time.<ref>{{cite book|editor-last=Porta|editor-first=M|title=A Dictionary of Epidemiology| chapter-url=https://books.google.com/books?id=okf1AwAAQBAJ&pg=PA69|year=2014|edition=5th|publisher=Oxford University Press|location=Oxford| isbn=978-0-19-939005-2|page=69|chapter=Death rate}}</ref>

The ''[[basic reproduction number]]'', ''R''<sub>0</sub>, is a statistical measure of the average number of people infected by a single infectious individual in a population with no prior immunity. If the basic reproduction number is less than 1, the epidemic will die out; if it is greater than 1, the epidemic will continues to spread—with exponential growth in the number of cases.<ref>{{cite web|title=Basic Reproductive Rate (Ro)|url=http://practice.sph.umich.edu/micphp/epicentral/basic_reproduc_rate.php|publisher=University of Michigan|access-date=16 September 2014|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141104222957/http://practice.sph.umich.edu/micphp/epicentral/basic_reproduc_rate.php|archive-date=4 November 2014|url-status=dead|df=dmy-all}}</ref> In September 2014, the estimated values of ''R''<sub>0</sub> were 1.71 (95%&nbsp;[[confidence interval|CI]], 1.44 to 2.01) for Guinea, 1.83 (95%&nbsp;CI, 1.72 to 1.94) for Liberia, and 2.02 (95%&nbsp;CI, 1.79 to 2.26) for Sierra Leone.<ref name=":1" /><ref>{{cite journal|last1=Althaus|first1=Christian L.|title=Estimating the Reproduction Number of Ebola Virus (EBOV) During the 2014 Outbreak in West Africa|journal=PLOS Currents|date=2014|doi=10.1371/currents.outbreaks.91afb5e0f279e7f29e7056095255b288|pmid=25642364|volume=6|pmc=4169395|bibcode=2014arXiv1408.3505A|arxiv=1408.3505}}</ref><ref>{{cite journal|last1=Hunt|first1=Allen G.|title=Exponential growth in Ebola outbreak since May 14, 2014|journal=Complexity|date=11 October 2014|volume=20|issue=2|pages=8–11|doi=10.1002/cplx.21615|bibcode=2014Cmplx..20b...8H}}</ref>
In October 2014, the WHO noted that exponential increase of cases continued in the three countries with the most intense transmission.<ref name=WHOvirtualpress>{{cite report|url=https://www.who.int/mediacentre/multimedia/vpc-23-october-2014.pdf?ua=1 |format= PDF|title=WHO Virtual Press Conference on the third meeting of the International Health Regulations Emergency Committee regarding the 2014 Ebola outbreak in West Africa|publisher=WHO|access-date=5 November 2014}}</ref>

=== Projections of future cases ===

On 28 August 2014, the WHO released its first estimate of the possible total cases from the outbreak as part of its road map for stopping the transmission of the virus. It stated that "this Roadmap assumes that in many areas of intense transmission the actual number of cases may be two- to fourfold higher than that currently reported. It acknowledges that the aggregate case load of Ebola could exceed 20,000 over the course of this emergency. The Roadmap assumes that a rapid escalation of the complementary strategies in intense transmission, resource-constrained areas will allow the comprehensive application of more standard containment strategies within three months." The report included an assumption that some country or countries would pay the required cost of their plan, estimated at half a billion US dollars.<ref name=res/>

When the WHO released these estimates, a number of epidemiologists presented data to show that the WHO projection of a total of 20,000 cases was likely an underestimate.<ref>{{cite web|last1=Kupferschmidt|first1=Kai|title=Disease modelers project a rapidly rising toll from Ebola|url=http://news.sciencemag.org/health/2014/08/disease-modelers-project-rapidly-rising-toll-ebola|publisher=Science (news)|access-date= 11 April 2015|date=2014-08-31}}</ref><ref>{{cite journal |title=Early Transmission Dynamics of Ebola Virus Disease (EVD), West Africa, March to August 2014 |journal=Eurosurveillance | date=11 September 2014 | author=Nishiura, H | author2=Chowell, G | volume=19 | issue=36 |pages=20894 |doi=10.2807/1560-7917.ES2014.19.36.20894 |pmid=25232919 |doi-access=free }}</ref> On 9 September, Jonas Schmidt-Chanasit of the [[Bernhard Nocht Institute for Tropical Medicine]] in Germany, controversially announced that the containment fight in Sierra Leone and Liberia had already been "lost" and that the disease would "burn itself out".<ref>{{cite news|title=Virologist: Fight against Ebola in Sierra Leone and Liberia is lost|url=http://www.dw.de/virologist-fight-against-ebola-in-sierra-leone-and-liberia-is-lost/a-17915090|date=11 September 2014|access-date=11 September 2014|publisher=Deutsche Welle}}</ref>

On 23 September 2014, the WHO revised their previous projection, stating that they expected the number of Ebola cases in Western Africa to be in excess of 20,000 by 2 November 2014.<ref name=":1" /> They further stated, that if the disease was not adequately contained it could become native in Guinea, Sierra Leone and Liberia, "spreading as routinely as malaria or the flu",<ref>{{cite news|last1=Szabo|first1=Liz|title=Could the Ebola outbreak last forever?|url=https://www.usatoday.com/story/news/nation/2014/09/22/ebola-last-forever/16072019/|newspaper=USA Today|access-date=22 September 2014|date=22 September 2014}}</ref> and according to an editorial in the [[New England Journal of Medicine]], eventually to other parts of Africa and beyond.<ref name="The Ebola Emergency — Immediate Action, Ongoing Strategy">{{cite journal|title=The Ebola Emergency — Immediate Action, Ongoing Strategy |journal=New England Journal of Medicine |author=Farrar, Jeremy J. |date=23 September 2014 |doi=10.1056/NEJMe1411471 |pmid=25244185 |volume=371 |issue = 16|pages=1545–1546|url=https://researchonline.lshtm.ac.uk/1947014/1/nejme1411471.pdf }}</ref>

In a report released on 23 September 2014, the CDC analysed the impact of under-reporting, which required correction of case numbers by a factor of up to 2.5. With this correction factor, approximately 21,000 total cases were estimated for the end of September 2014 in Liberia and Sierra Leone alone. The same report predicted that total cases, including unreported cases, could reach 1.4 million in Liberia and Sierra Leone by the end of January 2015 if no improvement in intervention or community behaviour occurred.<ref name="CDC Estimating future number of cases" /> However, at a congressional hearing on 19 November, the Director of the CDC said that the number of Ebola cases was no longer expected to exceed 1 million, moving away from the [[worst-case scenario]] that had been previously predicted.<ref>{{cite news|title=Overnight Health: Congress begs for attention on Ebola|url=http://thehill.com/policy/healthcare/overnights/224766-overnight-health-congress-begs-for-attention-on-ebola|newspaper=The Hill|access-date=20 November 2014|date=2014-11-19}}</ref>

A study published in December 2014 found that transmission of the Ebola virus occurs principally within families, in hospitals and at funerals. The data, gathered during three weeks of contact tracing in August, showed that the third person in any transmission chain often knew both the first and second person. The authors estimated that between 17% and 70% of cases in Western Africa were unreported—far fewer than had been estimated in prior projections. The study concluded that the epidemic would not be as difficult to control as feared, if rapid, vigorous contact tracing and quarantines were employed.<ref name=NYT_fewer_unreported>{{cite news|title=Fewer Ebola Cases Go Unreported Than Thought, Study Finds|url=https://www.nytimes.com/2014/12/16/science/fewer-ebola-cases-go-unreported-than-thought-study-finds-.html?_r=0|date=16 December 2014 |access-date= 11 April 2015|newspaper=The New York Times|first=Donald G.|last=McNeil Jr}}</ref>

Projections of future cases should also reflect the possibility that [[deforestation]] might have a hand in terms of the more recent Ebola outbreaks. It has been suggested that due to the clearing of forest for commercial use, various types of bats namely '' fruit bats'' may be taken out of their natural habitat and therefore into closer and potential contact with civilisation.<ref name="defor2">{{cite journal |last1=Nasi |first1=Robert |last2=Sheil |first2=Douglas |last3=Leendertz |first3=Siv Aina |last4=King |first4=Shona |last5=Suter |first5=Jamison |last6=Park |first6=Douglas |last7=Salim |first7=Mohammad A. |last8=Gaveau |first8=David |last9=Vargas |first9=J. Mario |last10=Farfán |first10=Miguel A. |last11=Márquez |first11=Ana L. |last12=Real |first12=Raimundo |last13=Fa |first13=John E. |last14=Olivero |first14=Jesús |title=Recent loss of closed forests is associated with Ebola virus disease outbreaks |journal=Scientific Reports |date=30 October 2017 |volume=7 |issue=1 |pages=14291 |doi=10.1038/s41598-017-14727-9 |pmid=29085050 |pmc=5662765 |language=en |issn=2045-2322|bibcode=2017NatSR...714291O }}</ref><ref name="defor">{{cite journal |last1=D’Odorico |first1=Paolo |last2=Hayman |first2=David T. S. |last3=Santini |first3=Monia |last4=Rulli |first4=Maria Cristina |title=The nexus between forest fragmentation in Africa and Ebola virus disease outbreaks |journal=Scientific Reports |date=14 February 2017 |volume=7 |pages=41613 |doi=10.1038/srep41613 |pmid=28195145 |pmc=5307336 |language=en |issn=2045-2322|bibcode=2017NatSR...741613R }}</ref>

== Economic effects ==
[[File:UNDG.gif|thumb|UNDG, improves efficiency in needing countries]]
In addition to the loss of life, the outbreak had a number of significant economic impacts.  In March 2015, the United Nations Development Group reported that due to a decrease in trade, closing of borders, flight cancellations, and drop in foreign investment and tourism activity fuelled by stigma, the epidemic resulted in vast economic consequences both in the affected areas and throughout Africa.<ref name="auto2" />  A September 2014 report in the ''[[Financial Times]]'' suggested that the economic impact of the Ebola outbreak could kill more people than the disease itself.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.australiannews.net/index.php/sid/225466201|publisher=Australian News.Net|title=IMF says Ebola impacted West African nations may need economic assistance}}</ref>

With regard to Ebola and economic activity in the country of Liberia, a study found that 8% of automotive firms, 8% of construction firms, 15% of food businesses and 30% of restaurants had closed due to the Ebola outbreak. Montserrado county experienced up to 20% firm closure. This indicated a decline in the Liberian national economy during the outbreak, as well as an indication that the county of Montserrado was hardest hit economically. The capital city Monrovia suffered construction and restaurant unemployment the most, while outside the capital, the food and beverage sectors suffered economically. A recuperation in the economy, at the end of the outbreak, was expected to be more rapid in some sectors than in others. Also, if the massive decline in economic activity persisted, the authors suggested a focus on economic recovery in addition to support for the healthcare system. The [[World Bank]] had projected an estimated loss of $1.6 billion in productivity for all three affected Western African countries combined for 2015. In Liberian counties that were less affected by the outbreak, the number of individuals employed fell by 24%. Montserrado saw a 47% decline in employment per firm in contrast to what was obtained prior to the Ebola outbreak.<ref>{{cite journal|title=Ebola, jobs and economic activity in Liberia|journal=Journal of Epidemiology and Community Health|volume=70|issue=3|pages=271–277|author=Jeremy Bowles|doi=10.1136/jech-2015-205959|pmid=26438188|pmc=4789821|year=2016}}</ref>

Another study showed that the economic effect of the Ebola outbreak would be felt for years due to preexisting [[social vulnerability]]. The economic effects were being felt nationwide in Liberia, such as the termination of expansions in the mining business. Initial scenarios had placed expected economic losses at $25 billion; however subsequent World Bank estimates were much lower, at about 12% of the combined [[GDP]] of the 3 worst hit countries.<ref name="stanturf">{{cite journal|last1=Stanturf|first1=John A.|last2=Goodrick|first2=Scott L.|last3=Jr|first3=Melvin L. Warren|last4=Charnley|first4=Susan|last5=Stegall|first5=Christie M.|title=Social Vulnerability and Ebola Virus Disease in Rural Liberia|journal=PLOS ONE|date=1 September 2015|volume=10|issue=9|pages=e0137208|doi=10.1371/journal.pone.0137208|issn=1932-6203|pmid=26325519|pmc=4556488|bibcode=2015PLoSO..1037208S|doi-access=free}}</ref> The authors went on to state that social vulnerability has multiple factors and proposed a classification based on multiple variables instead of single indicators such as food insecurity or lack of hospitals, which were problems faced by rural Liberians. In spite of the end of civil violence since 2003 and inflows from international donors, the reconstruction of Liberia had been very slow and non-productive—water delivery systems, sanitation facilities and centralised electricity were practically non-existent, even in Monrovia. Even before the outbreak, medical facilities did not have potable water, lighting or refrigeration. The authors indicated that lack of food and other economic effects would probably continue in the rural population long after the Ebola outbreak had ended.<ref name="stanturf" />

Other economic impacts were as follows:
* In August 2014 it was reported that many airlines had suspended flights to the area.<ref>{{cite news|author=Mark Anderson|url=https://www.theguardian.com/society/2014/aug/22/ebola-airlines-cancel-flights-guinea-liberia-sierra-leone|title=Ebola: airlines cancel more flights to affected countries|newspaper=The Guardian |date=22 August 2014|access-date=25 August 2014}}</ref>  Markets and shops had closed due to travel restrictions, a ''cordon sanitaire'', or fear of human contact, which led to loss of income for producers and traders.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.npr.org/2014/07/17/332351578/ebola-wreaks-economic-woe-in-west-africa |title=Ebola Wreaks Economic Woe In West Africa |newspaper=NPR.org |publisher=NPR |date=17 July 2014 |access-date=25 August 2014}}</ref>
* Movement of people away from affected areas disturbed agricultural activities.<ref>{{cite web|last=Lomax |first=Selma |url=http://allafrica.com/stories/201408200742.html |title=Liberia: Ebola Outbreak Affects Agriculture in Lofa County |publisher=allAfrica.com |date=20 August 2014 |access-date=25 August 2014}}</ref><ref name="rappler1">{{cite news|url=http://www.rappler.com/world/regions/africa/67128-who-un-first-staff-ebola-infection |title=First WHO worker infected by Ebola, DR Congo reports cases |work=Rappler.com |date=15 August 2014 |access-date=25 August 2014}}</ref> The FAO warned that the outbreak could endanger harvests and food security in Western Africa,<ref>{{cite news |url=http://uk.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-food-idUKKBN0GX0HB20140902 |title=Ebola threatens food security in West Africa: FAO |access-date=3 September 2014 | work=Reuters |date=2 September 2014}}</ref> and that with all the quarantines and movement limitations placed on them, more than 1 million people could be food insecure by March 2015.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.voanews.com/content/ebola-food-security-17dec14/2562832.html|title=Ebola Threatens Millions With Food Insecurity|publisher=VOA|access-date=19 December 2014}}</ref> By 29 July, the World Bank had given 10,500 tons of maize and rice seed to the 3 hardest-hit countries to help them to rebuild their agricultural systems.<ref>{{cite journal|url=http://www.csmonitor.com/Business/The-Bite/2015/0729/How-West-Africa-is-ramping-up-food-security-after-Ebola-outbreak|title=How West Africa is ramping up food security after Ebola outbreak|journal=Christian Science Monitor|author=The Christian Science Monitor|publisher=The Christian Science Monitor|access-date=10 August 2015|date=2015-07-29}}</ref>
* Tourism was directly impacted in the affected countries.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://afkinsider.com/69855/ebola-virus-eats-west-africas-economic-growth/ |title=Ebola Virus Eats Into West Africa's Economic Growth |publisher=AFKInsider |date=21 August 2014 |access-date=25 August 2014}}</ref> In April 2014, Nigeria reported that 75% of hotel business had been lost due to fears of the outbreak;<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.cnbcafrica.com/video/?bctid=3807923516001 |title= Impact of Ebola on e-commerce in Nigeria|publisher=CNBC Africa}}</ref>  the limited Ebola outbreak had cost that country [[naira|₦]]8 billion.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://allafrica.com/stories/201504230728.html|title=Nigeria: Lagos Hotels Lose N8 Billion Over Ebola Outbreak|publisher=allAfrica.com|access-date=3 May 2015}}</ref>  Other African countries that were not directly affected by the virus also reported adverse effects on tourism.<ref>{{cite news|last=Brock |first=Joe |url=http://uk.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-africatourism-idUKKBN0GK1GG20140820 |title=Ebola fears slowing tourist flow to Africa |work=Reuters |date=20 August 2014 |access-date=25 August 2014}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.cnbcafrica.com/video/?bctid=3807923516001|title=Video: Impact of Ebola on e-commerce in Nigeria|publisher=CNBC Africa|access-date=16 December 2014}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.vanguardngr.com/2014/09/mixed-grill-businesses-ebola-impacts-economy/|title=Mixed grill for businesses as Ebola impacts economy|publisher=Vanguard News|access-date=16 December 2014|date=2014-09-15}}</ref> For example, in 2015, it was reported that Gambia's tourism had fallen below 50 per cent of its normal business during the same period the prior year,<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/business-30495101|title=The Gambia's tourist industry reels from Ebola fallout|work=BBC News|access-date=13 January 2015|date=2015-01-07|last1=Williams|first1=Briohny}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.spyghana.com/ebola-scares-tourist-from-cape-coast-castle-in-2014/|title=Ebola scares tourist from Cape Coast Castle in 2014|author=Ghana News Agency|date=24 February 2015|publisher=spyghana.com|access-date=28 February 2015}}</ref> Elmina Bay in Ghana had an 80% decrease in US tourism,<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.humanosphere.org/social-business/2015/05/tourists-abandon-ghana-despite-its-successes-against-ebola/|title=Tourists abandon Ghana despite its successes against Ebola|author=Tom Murphy|publisher=Humanosphere|access-date=10 May 2015|date=May 2015}}</ref> and Kenya,<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.standardmedia.co.ke/business/article/2000154024/ktb-releases-sh140-million-for-tourism-campaigns|title=Kenya Tourism Board releases Sh140 million for tourism campaigns|date=8 March 2015|publisher=Standard Digital News|access-date=8 March 2015}}</ref> Zimbabwe,<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.thestandard.co.zw/2015/03/29/tourism-receipts-down-3/|title=Tourism receipts down 3%|newspaper=The Standard|access-date=10 April 2015|date=2015-03-29}}</ref>  Senegal, Zambia, and Tanzania also reported a drop.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://mg.co.za/article/2015-04-15-africa-travel-week-focuses-on-sustainable-tourism|title=Africa Travel Week focuses on sustainable tourism|author=Sharon Gilbert-Rivett|publisher=The M&G Online|access-date=16 April 2015|date=15 April 2015}}</ref>
* Some foreign mining companies withdrew all non-essential personnel, deferred new investment, and cut back operations.<ref name="rappler1" /><ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-27675747 |title=UK employees leave Sierra Leone over Ebola threat |work=BBC News |date=3 June 2014 |access-date=25 August 2014}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.mining.com/west-africa-ebola-outbreak-forces-miners-to-lock-down-operations-delay-projects-69246/ |title=West Africa Ebola outbreak forces miners to lock down operations, delay projects |publisher=MINING.com |date=10 August 2014 |access-date=25 August 2014}}</ref> In December 2014, it was reported that the iron ore mining company, African Minerals, had started the shutdown of its Sierra Leone operations because it was running low on income.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.theafricareport.com/West-Africa/mining-african-minerals-begins-shutdown-of-sierra-leone-assets-as-ebola-effect-hits.html|title=Mining: African Minerals begins shutdown of Sierra Leone assets as Ebola effect hits|author=Jun Feng|publisher=The Africa Report |access-date=21 December 2014|date=December 2014}}</ref> In March 2015, it was reported that Sierra Leone had begun to diversify away from mining, due to the country's recent problems.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/global-development/2015/mar/10/ebola-crisis-sierra-leone-diversify-mining-economy|title=Ebola crisis could force Sierra Leone to diversify economy away from mining – Clár Ní Chonghaile|author=Clár Ní Chonghaile|newspaper=The Guardian|access-date=27 March 2015|date=2015-03-10}}</ref>

In January 2015, [[Oxfam]], a UK-based disaster relief organisation, indicated that a "[[Marshall Plan]]" (a reference to the massive plan to rebuild Europe after [[World War&nbsp;II]]) was needed so that countries could begin to financially assist those that had been worst hit by the virus.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-30995631|title=Ebola crisis: Oxfam calls for recovery Marshall Plan|work=BBC News|access-date=27 January 2015|date=2015-01-27}}</ref>  The call was repeated in April 2015 when the most-affected Western African countries asked for an $8 billion "Marshall Plan" to rebuild their economies.  Speaking at the World Bank and the [[International Monetary Fund]] (IMF), Liberian president Ellen Johnson Sirleaf said the amount was needed because "[o]ur health systems collapsed, investors left our countries, revenues declined and spending increased."<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.newvision.co.ug/news/667165-ebola-hit-countries-call-for-8-bn-aid-plan.html|title=Ebola-hit countries call for $8 bn aid plan|publisher=newvision.co.ug|access-date=19 April 2015|archive-date=18 April 2015|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150418133519/http://www.newvision.co.ug/news/667165-ebola-hit-countries-call-for-8-bn-aid-plan.html|url-status=dead}}</ref>

The IMF has been criticised for its lack of assistance in the efforts to combat the epidemic.  In December 2014, a [[Cambridge University]] study linked IMF policies with the financial difficulties that prevented a strong Ebola response in the three most heavily affected countries,<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/worldnews/ebola/11307252/IMF-policies-blamed-for-weak-Ebola-response.html |archive-url=https://ghostarchive.org/archive/20220112/https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/worldnews/ebola/11307252/IMF-policies-blamed-for-weak-Ebola-response.html |archive-date=12 January 2022 |url-access=subscription |url-status=live|title=IMF policies blamed for weak Ebola response|date=22 December 2014|newspaper=The Telegraph |access-date=23 December 2014|location=London}}{{cbignore}}</ref> and they were urged by both the UN and NGOs who had worked in the affected countries to grant debt relief rather than low-interest loans. According to one advocacy group, "...&nbsp;yet the IMF, which has made a $9 billion surplus from its lending over the last three years, is considering offering loans, not debt relief and grants, in response".<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.businessghana.com/portal/news/index.php?op=getNews&id=202154|title=Ghana, Business Advice, Jobs, News, Business Directory, Real Estate, Finance, Forms, Auto|publisher=Business Ghana |access-date=20 December 2014}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.timeslive.co.za/africa/2014/12/31/imf-under-pressure-to-cancel-ebola-hit-countries-debts|title=IMF under pressure to cancel Ebola-hit countries' debts|author=AFP|publisher=Times LIVE|access-date=5 January 2015}}</ref> On 30 January 2015, the IMF reported it was close to reaching a deal on debt forgiveness.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://af.reuters.com/article/topNews/idAFKBN0L31AL20150130|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150202215207/http://af.reuters.com/article/topNews/idAFKBN0L31AL20150130|url-status=dead|archive-date=2 February 2015|title=IMF hopes for deal soon on debt forgiveness for Ebola countries|work=Reuters |access-date=3 February 2015|date=30 January 2015}}</ref> On 22 December, it was reported that the IMF had given Liberia an additional $10 million due to the economic impact of the Ebola virus outbreak.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.publicfinanceinternational.org/news/2015/12/imf-increases-support-liberian-economy-following-ebola-outbreak|title=IMF increases support to Liberian economy following Ebola outbreak|publisher=Public Finance International |access-date=14 January 2016}}</ref>

In October 2014, a World Bank report estimated overall economic impacts of between $3.8 billion and $32.6 billion, depending on the extent of the outbreak and speed of containment. It expected the most severe losses in the three affected countries, with a wider impact across the broader Western African region.<ref>{{cite news|title=World Bank rings alarm over £20bn worst-case Ebola scenario|url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/finance/economics/11149117/World-Bank-rings-alarm-over-20bn-worst-case-Ebola-scenario.html |archive-url=https://ghostarchive.org/archive/20220112/https://www.telegraph.co.uk/finance/economics/11149117/World-Bank-rings-alarm-over-20bn-worst-case-Ebola-scenario.html |archive-date=12 January 2022 |url-access=subscription |url-status=live|newspaper=The Telegraph|access-date=8 October 2014|location=London|first=James|last=Titcomb|date=8 October 2014}}{{cbignore}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|title=New World Bank Group Study Forecasts Billions in Economic Loss|url=http://www.worldbank.org/en/news/press-release/2014/10/08/ebola-new-world-bank-group-study-forecasts-billions-in-economic-loss-if-epidemic-lasts-longer-spreads-in-west-africa|publisher=World Bank|access-date=8 October 2014}}</ref>  On 13 April 2015, the World Bank said that they would soon announce a major new effort to rebuild the economies of the three hardest-hit countries.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://allafrica.com/stories/201504131122.html|title=Liberia: Rebuilding Ebola-Hit Economy – Focus of World Bank Spring Event|publisher=allAfrica.com|access-date=16 April 2015}}</ref> On 23 July, a World Bank poll warned that "we are not ready for another Ebola outbreak".<ref>{{cite magazine|url=http://time.com/3967662/world-bank-ebola-epidemics/|title='We Are Not Prepared For Another Epidemic': World Bank Survey|author=Alexandra Sifferlin|magazine=Time|access-date=3 August 2015|date=23 July 2015}}</ref> On 15 December, the World Bank indicated that by 1 December 2015, it had marshalled $1.62 billion in financing for the Ebola outbreak response.<ref>{{cite news| url=http://www.worldbank.org/en/topic/health/brief/world-bank-group-ebola-fact-sheet | title=World Bank Group Ebola Response Fact Sheet | date=15 December 2015 | publisher=World Bank | access-date=20 December 2015 }}</ref>

On 6 July 2015, UN Secretary-General [[Ban Ki-moon]] announced that he would host an Ebola recovery conference to raise funds for reconstruction, stating that the three countries hardest hit by Ebola needed about $700 million to rebuild their health services over a two-year period.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-funding-idUSKCN0PG29E20150706|title=Ebola-stricken nations need $700 million to rebuild healthcare|work=Reuters|access-date=8 July 2015|first=Daniel|last=Flynn|date=6 July 2015}}</ref> On 10 July, it was announced that the countries most affected by the Ebola epidemic would receive $3.4 billion to rebuild their economies.<ref>{{cite news |title=Ebola-Hit Countries Get Pledges of $3.4 Billion to Rebuild - ABC News |url=https://abcnews.go.com/US/wireStory/ebola-hit-countries-appeal-32-billion-rebuild-32360498 |access-date=27 March 2019 |work=web.archive.org |date=11 July 2015|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150711224812/https://abcnews.go.com/US/wireStory/ebola-hit-countries-appeal-32-billion-rebuild-32360498 |archive-date=11 July 2015 }}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.wsj.com/articles/donors-pledge-3-4-billion-for-ebola-recovery-plan-1436642289|title=Donors Pledge $3.4 Billion for Ebola Recovery Plan|author=Betsy McKay|date=11 July 2015|newspaper=The Wall Street Journal}}</ref> On 29 September, the leaders of both Sierra Leone and Liberia indicated at the [[UN General Assembly]] the launch of a "Post-Ebola Economic Stabilization and Recovery Plan".<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.un.org/apps/news/story.asp?NewsID=52049#.VhED7jZdFpj|title=United Nations News Centre|date=29 September 2015|publisher=UN News Service Section|access-date=4 October 2015}}</ref> On 24 November, it was reported that due to the decrease in commodity prices and the Western African Ebola epidemic, [[Africa–China relations|China's investment]] in the continent had declined 43% in the first 6&nbsp;months of 2015.<ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.irishtimes.com/business/economy/china-s-investment-in-africa-down-43-1.2440899|title=China's investment in Africa down 43%|date=24 November 2015|newspaper=The Irish Times|access-date=7 December 2015}}</ref> On 25 January, the IMF projected a GDP growth of 0.3% for Liberia, that country indicating it would cut spending by 11 per cent due to a stagnation in the mining sector, which would cause a domestic revenues drop of $57 million.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/liberia-budget-idUSL8N1594SV|title=Update 1 – Liberia to slash spending 11 pct amid mining slowdown|date=25 January 2016|work=Reuters |access-date=9 June 2016}}</ref>

== Responses ==

{{Further|Responses to the Ebola virus epidemic in West Africa}}

In July 2014, the WHO convened an emergency meeting of health ministers from eleven countries and announced collaboration on a strategy to co-ordinate technical support to combat the epidemic. In August they published a road map to guide and coordinate the international response to the outbreak, aiming to stop ongoing Ebola transmission worldwide within 6–9 months, and formally designated the outbreak as a [[Public Health Emergency of International Concern]].<ref name=WHO_PHEIC_8Aug2014 /> This is a legal designation used only twice before (for the [[2009 flu pandemic|2009 H1N1 (swine flu) pandemic]] and the 2014 resurgence of poliomyelitis) that invokes legal measures on disease prevention, surveillance, control, and response, by 194 signatory countries.<ref name="Reuters20k">{{cite news |url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-who-plan-idUSKBN0GS0XC20140828|title=WHO says Ebola outbreak could strike 20,000 people|work=Reuters|date=28 August 2014|access-date=28 August 2014}}</ref><ref name="urlInside Justice-WHO-PHEIC">{{cite news|url=http://www.insidejustice.com/intl/2009/04/30/swine_flu_who_pheic/ |publisher=Inside Justice |title=Swine Flu: Legal Obligations and Consequences When the WHO Declares a "Public Health Emergency of International Concern" |first=Renée |last=Dopplick |date=29 April 2009 |access-date=6 June 2014}}</ref>

In September 2014, the [[United Nations Security Council]] declared the Ebola virus outbreak in Western Africa "a threat to international peace and security" and unanimously adopted a resolution urging UN member states to provide more resources to fight the outbreak.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/world-africa-29262968|title=Ebola 'threat to world security'- UN Security Council|date=18 September 2014|work=BBC News|access-date=18 September 2014}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=http://thehill.com/policy/healthcare/217853-who-warns-ebola-to-cost-to-1-billion |title=WHO warns Ebola response could cost $1B|newspaper=The Hill |date=16 September 2014 |access-date=16 September 2014}}</ref>  In October, WHO and the UN Mission for Ebola Emergency Response announced a comprehensive 90-day plan to control and reverse the Ebola epidemic. The ultimate goal was to have capacity in place for the isolation of 100% of Ebola cases and the safe burial of 100% of casualties by 1 January 2015 (the 90-day target).<ref name="Ebola_Outbreak_total_WHO_15_Oct">{{cite report|title=Ebola Response Roadmap Situation Report 15 October 2014|url=http://apps.who.int/iris/bitstream/10665/136508/1/roadmapsitrep15Oct2014.pdf?ua=1 |format= PDF|publisher=WHO|access-date=29 October 2014|page=5}}</ref> Many nations and charitable organisations cooperated to realise the plan,<ref>{{cite web|title=Global Response|url=https://www.un.org/ebolaresponse/response.shtml|publisher=United Nations Mission for Ebola Emergency Response|access-date=29 October 2014}}</ref> and a WHO situation report published mid-December indicated that the international community was on track to meet the 90-day target.<ref>{{cite report|title=Ebola response roadmap situation report 17 December 2014|url=https://www.who.int/csr/disease/ebola/situation-reports/en/?m=20141217|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141219142704/http://www.who.int/csr/disease/ebola/situation-reports/en/?m=20141217|url-status=dead|archive-date=19 December 2014|publisher=WHO|access-date=19 December 2014}}</ref>

In May 2015, Dr Margaret Chan indicated, "demands on WHO were more than ten times greater than ever experienced in the almost 70-year history of this Organization"<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.internationalsos.com/ebola/index.cfm?content_id=407&language_id=ENG|title=Ebola|publisher=internationalsos.com|access-date=22 May 2015}}</ref><ref>Bruce-Cunning, Nick. (18 May 2015). [https://www.nytimes.com/2015/05/19/world/africa/who-leader-outlines-changes-response-ebola-epidemic.html?_r=0 WHO Leader Outlines Changes in Response to Ebola Epidemic]. ''The New York Times''.</ref> and on 23 March, she stated that "the world remains woefully ill-prepared to respond to outbreaks that are both severe and sustained."<ref>{{cite report|title=WHO Director-General addresses high-level conference on global health security|url=https://www.who.int/dg/speeches/2016/global-health-security/en/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160329094041/http://www.who.int/dg/speeches/2016/global-health-security/en/|url-status=dead|archive-date=29 March 2016|publisher=WHO|access-date=28 October 2016|ref=WHO}}</ref>

=== Criticism of WHO ===
[[File:Flag of WHO.svg|thumb|WHO flag]]
There was significant criticism of the [[WHO]] from some aid agencies because its response was perceived as slow and insufficient, especially during the early stages of the outbreak.<ref name="AP News" /><ref name="uk.reuters.com">{{cite news|title=Aid workers ask where was WHO in Ebola outbreak|url=http://uk.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-who-idUKKCN0HU03Q20141005|work=Reuters|access-date=5 October 2014|date=5 October 2014}}</ref> In October 2014, the [[Associated Press]] reported in an internal draft document that the WHO admitted  "nearly everyone" involved in the Ebola response failed to notice factors that turned the outbreak into the largest on record, and that they had missed chances to stop the spread of Ebola due to "incompetent staff, bureaucracy and a lack of reliable information".<ref>{{cite news|title=WHO chief promises transparency on Ebola failures|url=http://bigstory.ap.org/article/98181b6515da4baa94e6ac53ce880c07/who-chief-promises-transparency-ebola-failures|publisher=Associated Press|access-date=31 December 2014|archive-date=1 January 2015|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150101005020/http://bigstory.ap.org/article/98181b6515da4baa94e6ac53ce880c07/who-chief-promises-transparency-ebola-failures|url-status=dead}}</ref> Peter Piot, co-discoverer of the Ebola virus, called the WHO regional office in Africa "really not competent."<ref name="The Guardian">{{cite news| url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2014/oct/18/oxfam-call-for-troops-ebola--west-africa-world-health-organisation-accused | title=Oxfam calls for troops in Africa as criticism of 'inadequate' Ebola response mounts | newspaper=The Guardian | date=18 October 2014 | access-date=24 October 2014 | author=McVeigh, Tracy}}</ref> In April 2015, the WHO admitted very serious failings in handling the crisis and indicated reforms for any future crises; "we did not work effectively in coordination with other partners, there were shortcomings in risk communications and there was confusion of roles and responsibilities".<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-health-ebola-who-idUSKBN0NA12J20150419|title=WHO leadership admits failings over Ebola, promises reform|work=Reuters|access-date=16 January 2016}}</ref> The Ebola crisis was discussed at the June 2015 [[G7 (major advanced economies)|G7 meeting]].  The leaders pledged to assist in carrying out WHO regulations. Critics criticised the G7 leaders, saying they were not committed enough in the fight against the possibility of future pandemics.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/may/18/angela-merkel-plan-restructure-world-health-organisation|title=Plan to reform WHO after Ebola to be unveiled by Angela Merkel|author=Sarah Boseley|newspaper=The Guardian|access-date=19 May 2015|date=2015-05-18}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.politico.eu/article/g7-ebola-fight-response-ngo-leaders-reactions/|title=G7 underwhelms with Ebola response|publisher=Politico|access-date=17 June 2015|date=2015-06-08}}</ref>

In 2015 a panel of experts looked at the ways of preventing small outbreaks from becoming large epidemics. Their recommendations were published in the November issue of ''[[The Lancet]]''.  According to the panel, the epidemic had exposed problems in the national (and international) institutions responsible for protecting the public from the human consequences of infectious disease outbreaks such as the Ebola epidemic.  The panel was highly critical of the WHO's management of the Ebola crisis noting that it took them months to respond and when they did they were slow to act, poorly co-ordinated and inadequately informed.  The report pointed out that the committee responsible for checking the WHO's actions during the outbreak (i.e. the WHO Ebola Interim Assessment Panel) had delayed responses due to worries about political resistance from the Western African leaders, economic consequences, and a system within the WHO that discouraged open debate about issues such as emergency declarations. The WHO may also have hesitated because it was criticised for creating panic by declaring a public health emergency during the relatively mild [[2009 H1N1 pandemic]]. This, the report states, showed the risks in having such consequential decision-making power in one individual—a risk made worse when there was no mechanism of responsibility for such leadership failure.<ref name="lancet">{{cite journal|url= |title=Will Ebola change the game? Ten essential reforms before the next pandemic. The report of the Harvard-LSHTM Independent Panel on the Global Response to Ebola|journal=The Lancet|doi=10.1016/S0140-6736(15)00946-0|pmid=26615326|volume=386|issue=10009|pages=2204–2221|year=2015|last1=Moon|first1=Suerie|last2=Sridhar|first2=Devi|last3=Pate|first3=Muhammad A|last4=Jha|first4=Ashish K|last5=Clinton|first5=Chelsea|last6=Delaunay|first6=Sophie|last7=Edwin|first7=Valnora|last8=Fallah|first8=Mosoka|last9=Fidler|first9=David P|last10=Garrett|first10=Laurie|last11=Goosby|first11=Eric|last12=Gostin|first12=Lawrence O|last13=Heymann|first13=David L|last14=Lee|first14=Kelley|last15=Leung|first15=Gabriel M|last16=Morrison|first16=J Stephen|last17=Saavedra|first17=Jorge|last18=Tanner|first18=Marcel|last19=Leigh|first19=Jennifer A|last20=Hawkins|first20=Benjamin|last21=Woskie|first21=Liana R|last22=Piot|first22=Peter|pmc=7137174|doi-access=free}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=http://www.cnn.com/2015/11/23/africa/ebola-lancet-report/|title=Ebola crisis: WHO slammed by Harvard-convened panel over slow response|publisher=CNN|date=23 November 2015|access-date=23 November 2015}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|title=Ebola global response was 'too slow', say health experts|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/health-34877787|access-date=23 November 2015|work=BBC News |date=23 November 2015}}</ref>

The panel outlined 10 recommendations for the prevention and handling of future infectious disease outbreaks.  Included in the recommendations of the changes needed to fight future outbreaks is the creation of a U.N. Security Council health committee to expedite political attention to health issues and the establishment of a global fund to finance and accelerate the development of outbreak-relevant drugs and treatment.  The report also noted that competent governance of the global system demanded political leadership and a WHO that is more focused and appropriately financed and whose integrity is restored through the application of adequate reforms and leadership.<ref name="lancet"/>

The WHO also came under fire for refusing to send Dr. Olivet Buck to Germany for experimental treatment after she contracted Ebola per the government of Sierra Leone's request. WHO claimed they could only evacuate medical professionals they had deployed to the region, not locals. Dr. Olivet Buck was the Medical Superintendent at Lumley Government Hospital in Freetown, Sierra Leone. It's believed that she contracted the virus while continuing to treat patients even during a shortage of personal protective equipment. Her loyalty and dedication to her community never wavered.<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Green |first1=Andrew |title=Remembering health workers who died from Ebola in 2014 |journal=The Lancet |date=20 December 2014 |volume=384 |issue=9961 |pages=2201–2206 |doi=10.1016/S0140-6736(14)62417-X |pmid=25625389 |s2cid=23124192 |url=https://www.thelancet.com/journals/lancet/article/PIIS0140-6736(14)62417-X/fulltext |access-date=29 February 2020 |language=en |issn=0140-6736}}</ref>

==Notable patients==
* [[Patrick Sawyer]]—index case for Nigeria; died on 24 July 2014.<ref name=ibeh20140821>{{cite web|url=https://www.premiumtimesng.com/news/top-news/167114-ebola-lagos-hospital-which-treated-patrick-sawyer-to-receive-n4m.html|title=Ebola: Lagos hospital which treated Patrick Sawyer to receive N4m|author=Nnenna Ibeh|date=2014-08-21|publisher=The Premium Times}}</ref>
* [[Sheik Umar Khan]]—was a Sierra Leonean doctor, died on 29 July 2014.<ref name=heavytoll>{{cite web|title=Ebola's heavy toll on study authors|url=http://news.sciencemag.org/health/2014/08/ebolas-heavy-toll-study-authors|access-date=18 October 2014|date=2014-08-28}}</ref>
* [[Ameyo Adadevoh]]—curbed a wider spread of the virus in Nigeria; died on 19 August 2014.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://ynaija.com/site/breaking-dr-ameyo-adadevoh-dies-ebola-virus-infection/ |title=Dr Ameyo Adadevoh dies from Ebola virus infection YNaija l The Internet Newspaper for Young Nigerians |publisher=Ynaija.com |date= 2014-08-19|access-date=20 August 2014}}</ref>
* [[Thomas Eric Duncan]]—first Ebola patient diagnosed in U.S.; died on 8 October 2014.<ref name="ABC News">{{cite web|url=https://abcnews.go.com/Health/texas-ebola-patient-thomas-eric-duncan-died/story?id=26045360|title=Texas Ebola Patient Thomas Eric Duncan Has Died|work=ABC News|access-date=8 October 2014|date=2014-10-08}}</ref>
* [[Salome Karwah]]—survived Ebola in 2014 and became a nurse; named co-[[Time Person of the Year|Person of the Year]] by [[Time (magazine)|''Time'']] magazine; Karwah died of complications of childbirth in February 2017.<ref name=time>{{cite news |first=Aryn|last=Baker |title=Liberian Ebola Fighter, a TIME Person of the Year, Dies in Childbirth |url=http://time.com/4683873/ebola-fighter-time-person-of-the-year-salome-karwah/ |magazine=[[Time (magazine)|Time]] |date=2017-02-27 |access-date=2017-03-15}}</ref>
* [[Ian Crozier]]—contracted the disease in September 2014, was treated at Emory University from 9 September to 19 October of that year.<ref>{{cite news | url=http://www.latimes.com/nation/nationnow/la-na-nn-ebola-patients-us-20141208-story.html | title=With Ian Crozier's reveal, here are the 10 Ebola patients treated in U.S. | work=Los Angeles Times | date=8 December 2014 | access-date=8 May 2015 | author=Raab, Lauren}}</ref>
* [[Pauline Cafferkey]]—British nurse and aid worker, infected in 2014, recovered, but has subsequently been readmitted to hospital several times.<ref>{{cite news|last1=Nicolson|first1=Stuart|title=Profile: Ebola nurse Pauline Cafferkey|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-scotland-30684665|access-date=8 December 2016|work=BBC News|date=18 August 2016}}</ref>

==Literature==
* ''[[Fevers, Feuds, and Diamonds: Ebola and the Ravages of History]]''. [[Paul Farmer]]. New York: Farrar, Straus and Giroux, 2020. {{ISBN|978-0374234324}}

:Paul Farmer is a medical anthropologist and physician, and the co-founder of [[Partners in Health]].  Farmer first visited the Ebola outbreak site in July, 2014, and he devotes much of the book to his personal experiences.  Reviewing the outbreak in 2020, he noted that there were almost no Ebola deaths in the U.S. or Europe.  By Farmer's account, the West Africa Ebola death toll arose from the longstanding failure to invest in basic health infrastructure which resulted in a lack of proper medical care.  Looking at the history of West Africa, Farmer blames the almost five centuries of European rule that resulted in the "rapacious extraction — of rubber latex, timber, minerals, gold, diamonds and human chattel" for the country's inability to provide adequate health care.<ref>{{cite news |last1=Johnson |first1=Steven |title=The Deadliness of the 2014 Ebola Outbreak Was Not Inevitable |url=https://www.nytimes.com/2020/11/17/books/review/fevers-feuds-and-diamonds-paul-farmer.html |newspaper=The New York Times |date=17 November 2020 |access-date=18 February 2021}}</ref>
